He gave another short bow. “Our survey is complete. You will be notified of the council’s findings regarding the acoustic hygiene of van der Hagen Lutes. Good day.”

They left, closing the Red Door behind them with a soft, final click.

The silence they left in their wake was different. It was violated. They had taken our quiet and quantified it, stolen its privacy. Ewan emerged from behind the curtain, shaking. Lucas stared at the door, his thumb running over the edge of his wooden hook.

I went to the Lute’s case. I laid my hands on it. The pulse was there, but it felt agitated, a quickened heartbeat. As if it had sensed the alien listening.

“They heard nothing,” Ewan whispered. “They didn’t find me.”

“They weren’t listening for you, boy,” Lucas said, his voice grim. “They were listening for the shape of the hole you make in the world. The silence around a secret has its own pitch.” He looked at me. “They heard ours.”

The freedom of our constraint was gone. Thorne had not breached our walls; he had simply sent in his cartographers. They had drawn the lines of our resistance, the contours of our fear. They had identified the protected places—the storeroom, the case. Our harmony was now charted. The next move would not be measurement. It would be a finger, pressing precisely on a key.

That night, the gulls did not return. Their continued absence was a verdict.

I could not sleep. I sat in the workshop, the single candle guttering. The Lute’s case was on my lap. I understood now the true nature of the choice Lucas had posed. A defensive chord was impossible. We had already played it—our refusal, our unity, our fear. It was written on Arnot’s wax tablet. A melody to change the key of the room? What melody could possibly alter the calculus of a man who treated people as collections of resonant frequencies?

The truth was too bright, too potent. But perhaps brightness was not the answer. Not directly.

An idea, fragile and dissonant, began to form. It was not a melody. It was a discord. A purposeful, controlled wrongness.

If Thorne’s ears were tuned to find hidden harmonies, what if I presented him with a false one? A harmony so compelling, so obviously secret and powerful, it would draw all his attention? A decoy resonance, while the true note rested, silent and safe, elsewhere.

It would require a sacrifice. It would require crafting a lie of such acoustic perfection that it would feel truer than truth. It would mean making something beautiful, only to offer it up for destruction.

I looked at my father’s tools, gleaming in the candlelight. I looked at the shelves of rare woods, the drawers of gut and wire. I had the skill. I had the materials. I could build a ghost.

The constraint was absolute. We were mapped, listened to, surrounded. But within that cage, a new, terrible freedom opened before me. The freedom to create a masterpiece of deception. To compose a siren song for a man who collected silence, a song so convincing it would make him believe he had finally found the key to every song in the world.

I would build a second Lute.

Not a copy. An echo. An instrument that sang with a glorious, hollow truth. An instrument I would let him take.

The candle flame dipped, then steadied. In the new, settled light, I saw my path. It was a path into deeper shadow, a composition of lies. But it was the only counterpoint left to play.

Our quiet had been stolen. Now, I would give him a noise he would never forget.



---

# Chapter 15

## **CHAPTER 15**

The idea was a splinter under the skin of my mind. It did not fade with the dawn, nor soften with the grey light that seeped through the workshop shutters. It festered. It grew. By the time the bell of St. Giles tolled Terce, the fragile dissonance had hardened into a plan, a cold and intricate architecture of deceit.

My father moved through the morning like a man in a sealed glass jar. He polished finished instruments that needed no polish. He sorted piles of spruce by grain, his fingers tracing lines only he could see. The silence between us was no longer the quiet of shared dread, but the loud, humming void of separate calculations. He was thinking of defence, of walls. I was thinking of bait.

“The rosewood,” I said. My voice sounded strange in the thick air, a crack in the varnish of our quiet.

He looked up from a viol bridge he was not carving. “The Madagascar?”

“The one from the sea-chest. The piece with the flame figure.”

His eyes, the colour of old sherry, held mine. That wood was his treasure, bought a decade ago from a Levant merchant for a sum that had made my mother laugh with disbelief. It was for a masterpiece, he had said. For an instrument that would sing for a king.

“It is spoken for,” he said, the lie clumsy on his tongue. No commission hung on our board. No king awaited our work.

“It is needed,” I replied. I did not say for what. The workshop, we now knew, had ears. The very air might be carrying our words to the house on the Law. We spoke in the code of craft, in the shorthand of grain and glue. “The client requires a specific resonance. A depth. A certain… darkness in the bloom.”

He set down his knife. The *click* of steel on oak was a full stop. He understood. Not the details, but the category of action. The sacrifice. He walked to the iron-bound sea-chest in the corner, knelt with a soft groan of joints, and lifted the lid. The smell that rose was not of the sea, but of a forgotten forest, dense and spice-laden. He drew out the billet, wrapped in oilcloth. It was smaller than I remembered, a dense, dark heart no larger than my forearm.

He placed it on the bench between us, a priest offering a relic. “It will want a partner of equal arrogance. Spruce too humble will be devoured by it.”

“The Carpathian,” I said. “The one with the late-summer grain.”

A slow nod. That wood was my find, traded for a week’s labour tuning the harpsichord of a bankrupt earl. It was my treasure. We were to bankrupt ourselves, piece by precious piece, for this fiction.

The work began not with saw or plane, but with a ledger. I opened the great book where we recorded commissions, and I began to write a ghost.

*For the Honourable Member of the Burgesses, a lute after the model of Hieber. Body of Madagascar rosewood, table of Carpathian spruce. Purfling of bog oak and ivory. Twenty-one ribs. A voice of profound declamation, suited to the Italian style.*

I wrote the details with a steady hand, inventing a patron whose name I plucked from the civic rolls I had seen in the tolbooth. I described a sound—*sombre in the bass, with a weeping clarity in the treble*—and a delivery date six weeks hence. I sanded the page lightly at the edges, spilled a drop of weak ale to simulate a careless moment, let a fleck of ash from the brazier fall upon it. I created a history for the object that did not exist.

My father watched, his face a mask of grim comprehension. This was the melody to change the key of the room. A composition in vellum and ink.

“The ribs will be a challenge,” he said, his voice pitched for the walls. “The curvature is extreme in the Hieber model. We will need the hot sands.”

“I have prepared the former,” I said, gesturing to the stack of thin, resawn rosewood strips. “The sands are in the iron pot.”

And so we began to build a lie. We bent the ribs over the heated copper pipe, the wood groaning as it took the shape of a vessel that would never hold music, only the expectation of it. The smell of heated rosewood filled the workshop, a perfume of sweet corruption. Every stroke of the plane, every whisper of sandpaper, was a performance. We spoke of glue temperatures, of the alignment of the neck block, of the merits of rosewood versus maple for the bridge. We were two actors on a stage, playing to an unseen audience we knew was there, behind the eyes of the beggar across the street, in the rhythm of the watchman’s patrol.

At night, by the light of a single, shuttered candle in my garret, I worked on the true instrument. Not a lute, but a container. Lucas’s wax tablet, with its dangerous, luminous truth, could not stay hidden in a floorboard. It had to be made invisible in plain sight.

I took the old, failed viola da gamba belly—the one with the hidden wolf tone my father could never tame—from the scrap pile. With a fine gouge, I hollowed a shallow chamber in its thickness, behind the bass bar. The tablet fit inside like a heart in a ribcage. I sealed it with a patch of matching spruce, glued with fish glue that could be steamed open again. I re-varnished the patch, blending it into the old, ambered surface until the seam vanished to any eye but mine. The belly was a ruin, acoustically dead, useful only as firewood or, now, as a coffin for a secret.

The decoy lute grew. Its ribs closed into a perfect shell. We glued the delicate lining strips. We fitted the end block. It was a beautiful, hollow corpse. We gave it all the outward signs of life: perfect symmetry, exquisite joints, a sanded interior smooth as a river stone.

One afternoon, as I was fitting the temporary soundboard to check the bracing, the shop bell rang. Not the tentative tinkle of a customer, but a single, firm stroke.

Arnot stood in the doorway, the Haar fog clinging to the shoulders of his dun-coloured coat like mould. He did not step fully inside. His gaze swept the room, a slow, auditory scan. It lingered on the rosewood shell, clamped in its mould.

“Progress,” he stated. It was not a question.

“A commission,” my father said, his voice stripped of all inflection. “It demands our focus.”

Arnot’s eyes moved to the ledger, open on its stand. He took two steps forward, leaned over, and read the entry I had forged. His finger, blunt and clean, traced the line describing the patron. He said nothing for a long moment. The only sound was the hiss of the brazier.

“A specific voice,” he murmured, almost to himself. Then he looked directly at me. “Master Thorne is curious about specificity. He wonders what you hear, when you listen to a piece of wood. Not the note it will make, but the… intention within it.”

My blood became cold salt in my veins. “Intention is given by the maker,” I said.

“Is it?” A faint, humourless crease at the corner of his mouth. “Or is it merely uncovered? He requests your presence. Tomorrow, after Sext. He wishes to discuss resonance. In the abstract.”

It was not a request. I gave a single, stiff nod.

Arnot’s gaze returned to the nascent lute. “A fine choice. Rosewood of that density… it has a memory of its own. It does not forget the soil that grew it, or the axe that felled it. It sings of endings.” With that, he turned and left, the fog swallowing him whole.

The next day, I walked to the Law as if to the gallows. The house was not as I remembered it. Before, it had been a place of oppressive silence. Now, knowing its purpose, I heard the silence differently. It was not an absence, but a cultivated medium, like the gelatin in my father’s glue pot, waiting to capture an impression.

Thorne received me in a different room, a small, circular chamber on an upper floor. It was lined with books, not instruments. A single, thick-paned window looked out over the slate-grey roofscape towards the Cathedral. In the centre of the room was a table, and on the table sat a covered object.

“Isabeau,” he said. He wore a simple dark robe, his hands clasped before him. He looked like a scholar, not a collector. It was more frightening. “Thank you for coming. I find myself preoccupied with a theoretical problem. I thought a maker’s mind might provide a… practical lens.”

He gestured to the table. “An object of unknown provenance. I have had it for some years. I know what it is made of, how it was likely constructed. But its original song eludes me. Every analysis yields only the echo of its last, degraded state.”

He lifted the cover. It was a harp. A small, Gothic harp, its willow frame warped, its strings long gone. The soundboard was stained and cracked. It was a skeleton of music.

“I can map its resonant frequencies as it is,” he continued, circling the table. “I can tell you the exact pitch at which its spine would vibrate, the harmonic series of its dead cavity. But this is autopsy. It tells me nothing of the living sound. Was it bright? Was it melancholic? Did it accompany hymns, or bawdy songs?” He stopped and looked at me. “Can the wood remember?”

I approached the table. I did not touch the harp. I looked at the stains—water, wine, the ghost of fingerprints. “The wood remembers stress,” I said, my voice low. “It remembers damp, and heat, and the tension of the strings that pulled against it for decades. It remembers the shape of the hands that held it. But not the tune. The tune is a bird. It lands, it sings, it flies away. The wood is only the branch.”

He absorbed this, his head tilted. “A poetic deflection. But not, I think, an untrue one. The branch, however, determines the quality of the song. A stable branch. A resonant branch.” He moved to the window. “Your brother sought to record the bird. A futile, beautiful endeavour. To catch the flight itself. My interest is in the branch. In mapping every possible branch, so that when the bird lands, its song is… understood. Contained.”

He turned back, his eyes alight with a calm, terrifying fervour. “Your family are master carvers of branches. You understand the preparation, the shaping, the waiting. This is why you are here, and not in a cell. Your usefulness is potential. Your father’s refusal to collaborate is a miscalculation. Potential can be realised under many conditions. Duress is one of them.”

The threat hung in the still air, clearer than any shout.

“We are working,” I said. “The lute on our bench. It is a complex piece.”

“The rosewood lute,” he nodded. “For a burgess. Yes. I look forward to hearing its voice. I trust its song will be… worth the wait.” He let the silence stretch, a taut string. “You may go. Consider our conversation. Consider the difference between the branch and the bird. And consider which one you are ultimately shaping.”

The walk home was a blur of wet cobbles and echoing footsteps. His meaning was a clamp around my lungs. He knew the lute was a focus, a channel for our activity. He was allowing it, watching it. He was not interested in the burgess, or the commission. He was interested in what the making of it would reveal about us. He was studying our craft, our choices, as data. The decoy was already serving its purpose, but not as I had intended. It was not distracting him from the truth. It was feeding him a new kind of truth about the makers themselves.

When I returned, the workshop was empty. A note in my father’s hand lay on the bench beside the lute shell.

*Gone to the yards for spruce. The belly must be cut tomorrow.*

But it was the other thing on the bench that stopped my breath. Beside his note, sitting on a square of velvet, was the mother-of-pearl. The shell pieces I had been saving for a lifetime, for the ultimate rosette of the ultimate instrument. Iridescent, luminous, each fragment a captured moonbeam. I had never shown them to him. He must have found my hiding place.

He had left them out for the decoy.

It was the final, devastating commitment to the lie. To give the false instrument our most beautiful lie. To make it not just convincing, but irresistible. To make it hurt.

My hands shook as I picked up a piece. It was cool, its colours shifting from silver to deep blue as I turned it. To waste this on a sacrifice was a kind of blasphemy. It was also the only move left.

That night, I did not work on the hidden compartment. I sat on the floor of the workshop, in the dark, with the pearl shell and my finest saws. I cut the pieces into the intricate, interlocking geometric patterns of a Hieber rosette. Each tiny cut was a surrender. Each fragment, when glued into the soundboard of the decoy, would be a piece of my history given to the void.

I worked until my eyes burned and my fingers were numb. The false lute demanded truth, and so I gave it the truest thing I had. I adorned the ghost with my inheritance.

Three days later, we closed the box. The Carpathian spruce soundboard, with its glorious, pearl-studded rosette, was glued onto the rosewood shell. We clamped it with every cramp we owned, a legion of iron insects swarming the body. It sat in the centre of the workshop, a chrysalis.

We could only wait for the glue to cure. In that waiting, the tension in the house became a thing you could lean against. We spoke less and less. My father’s eyes followed me, filled with a question he would not voice: *Is it enough?*

I did not know.

Then, on the evening of the fourth day, as I was sweeping up curls of spruce, my foot caught on a loose floorboard near the wood store. Not my hiding place. Another one. I knelt, pried it up with a sense of dread.

Beneath it was not a secret, but an absence. A clean, empty space. And in that space, a single item. A small, crudely carved wooden bird, its wings folded. It was Lucas’s work, from when he was a boy. He had always been terrible at carving.

He had left it for me to find. A sign. The bird, not the branch.

But it was the other thing that turned the air in my lungs to ice. Beside the bird, pressed into the dust of the foundation, was a partial footprint. Too large to be Lucas’s. The tread was distinct, a pattern of circles and lines I had seen before, in the mud by our door after the first visit, and on the threshold of Thorne’s circular library.

Arnot had been here. He had found Lucas’s old cache. He had taken whatever was in it. And he had left the bird, a mocking echo of Thorne’s metaphor, to show me he knew.

The decoy lute, clamped and silent, seemed to mock me from its stand. We were building a beautiful trap, and they were already in the walls. Our performance was being watched from the wings. The controlled discord of my plan was spiralling into genuine chaos.

I placed the floorboard back. I picked up the wooden bird, its shape awkward in my palm. The hook for the next move was not of my making. It had been set for me. They were not waiting for the lute to be finished. They were waiting for me to understand how thoroughly I was already caught.

I looked at the gleaming pearl rosette, visible through the soundhole of the clamped instrument. My sacrifice, my beautiful lie. It was no longer a decoy. It was a clock, ticking down to a moment I could not yet see. And the footsteps in the dark beneath our floor told me I had less time than the glue would take to dry.



---

# Chapter 16

## **CHAPTER 16**

The wooden bird was a cold, clumsy weight against my skin. I slipped it into the inner pocket of my apron, where it lay against my ribs like a second, misshapen heart. The act of hiding it was instinct, a pointless secrecy. Arnot knew I would find it. The footprint was not a clue left in haste; it was a signature. He had signed the empty space beneath our floorboards, and the performance was now his to direct.

I finished sweeping the spruce shavings. Each curl was a fallen leaf from a tree I was methodically hollowing out. I banked the stove’s fire, my movements precise, a liturgy against the chaos. Upstairs, the murmur of Boethius had ceased. The silence from the solar was a different kind of reading, one my father had mastered: the text of my preoccupation, the margin notes of my fear.

He descended the stairs as I was wiping my hands on a rag. He did not ask about the floorboard. He looked at the decoy lute, clamped and silent on its stand, its pearl rosette a blind eye.

“The glue will hold best if left undisturbed another full day,” he said. His voice was the one he used for stating grain direction, for noting the humidity’s effect on spruce. “The wood must forget it was ever separate.”

“And if it is disturbed too soon?” I asked, not looking at him.

“The seam will hold, but it will always be a fault line. A place where the tone will fracture, under the right pressure.” He came to stand beside me, not touching, his presence a solid column in the unsettled air. “Isabeau. The wood knows the truth of the hand that shapes it. This instrument… it is full of truths you have not told me.”

I thought of the bird in my pocket, of Lucas’s terrible, earnest carving. I thought of the ledger, a phantom limb. “Some truths are not mine to tell. Some are just… absences.”

He absorbed this, his gaze on the false lute. “An absence has a shape. It carves the space around it. This work you are doing… it has the shape of a sacrifice. But a sacrifice requires an altar, and a priest. Be certain you know which one you are.”

He turned and walked back to the bench where a viol’s neck waited for fretting. The conversation was over. He had given me his reading: a fracture line, a sacrifice, a role to choose. It was more than I had given him.

The afternoon was a study in forced normalcy. I worked on a rebec, fitting its skin-thin soundboard. I answered the door to a merchant’s boy delivering a block of seasoned maple. I drank bitter tea and watched the light move across the floorboards, a slow tide that never quite reached the loose plank by the wood store. Every mundane action felt like a line spoken on a stage, with critics hidden in the gallery.

As dusk began to pool in the corners of the workshop, the expected sound came: three firm knocks at the Red Door. Not Arnot’s polite, insistent tap. This was heavier, declarative.

I opened it to find Captain de Vries on the step, the dying light catching the frost in his beard. He was not in his city guard regalia, but in a worn leather jerkin over a woolen tunic. A man off-duty, or a man wishing to appear so.

“Mistress van der Hagen. May I enter?”

He did not wait for a full answer, stepping across the threshold as if confirming a suspicion. His eyes performed a swift, professional inventory of the room: the tools in their racks, the instruments in their cradles, my father’s bent head at his bench, the prominent decoy on its stand.

“You are working late,” he observed.

“The work does not observe the clock,” I said, echoing my father’s old dictum. “Can I offer you wine?”

“No.” He moved to the center of the room, a rock in a stream, and the workshop flowed around his stillness. He looked directly at the decoy lute. “This is the commission? For the Lord Provost’s man?”

“It is.”

“It looks… complex.”

“All lutes are complex. They are marriages of tension and release.”

He grunted, unimpressed by poetry. “A marriage requires trust. I find myself questioning where that trust resides.” He turned from the instrument to me. “Your brother. Lucas. When did you last see him?”

The question was a blade, clean and cold. I met his gaze. “Weeks ago. Before the first visit from Master Arnot.”

“And you have had no word? No letter, no message passed through the taverns of the Haven?”

“If I had,” I said, my voice flattening to the pitch of the truth, “I would not be sweeping my floor in the hope of finding a clue under the boards.”

He studied me, his eyes the colour of a winter harbour. “A curious turn of phrase.” He took a slow pace towards the wood store, his boot heels firm on the oak. He stopped a hand’s breadth from the loose plank. My blood became a silent hum in my ears. “The city is a body, mistress. It has its humours. Its fevers. Right now, there is a fever in the shadows around the customs house. Whispers of missing records. Of a ledger that did not burn as completely as some had hoped.”

He did not look at me. He was speaking to the room, to the viols hanging like listening ears.

“I am a maker of musical instruments, Captain. I know nothing of ledgers.”

“But you know of pressure,” he said, turning back. “The pressure of a string on a bridge. The pressure of a debt on a family. The pressure of a secret on a soul.” He took a step closer. His voice dropped, not to a whisper, but to the low, carrying tone of a confession. “Arnot is not what he seems. His authority is a borrowed cloak. There are other men, men with less polish and more reach, who are also interested in this… ledger. They are not patient men. They do not appreciate theatricality.”

He was warning me. But from whom? From Arnot? Or for Arnot?

“Why are you telling me this?”

“Because the body of the city is also my body. A fever in one limb weakens the whole. And because,” he paused, his jaw tightening, “I knew your mother. She had a clear tone. I hear too much discord now.”

The mention of her was a master stroke. It bypassed all my defences, ringing a note so pure and unexpected it left me silent.

“Finish your commission, Mistress van der Hagen. Deliver it cleanly. Do not look for Lucas in the shadows. The shadows here are deeper than you know.” His eyes flicked once more to the loose floorboard, a barely perceptible acknowledgement. “And fix that. A tripping hazard in a workshop is a foolish risk.”

He left as solidly as he had arrived, closing the door with a finality that shuddered through the iron lion’s mouth.

My father had not stirred from his bench, but his plane was still on the wood. He was a statue of a man listening.

“He knows,” I said, the words ash in my mouth.

“He knows there is a game,” my father corrected softly, without turning. “He does not know the rules, or the players. He is a man trying to keep the board from being overturned.” He picked up his plane, tested its edge with his thumb. “A warning from a guardsman is a currency. It must be spent, or it devalues. He has spent it here.”

“On us.”
“On the possibility of us,” he said. “Now you must decide what possibility you will show him.”

I spent the evening in a state of heightened stillness. I did not look at the floorboard. I did not touch the wooden bird. I tended the stove, I trimmed a set of lute strings, I pretended to read a treatise on the properties of willow. I was waiting for the next move, the hook Arnot had set.

It came with the full dark.

A different knock. A rapid, light tattoo, like a bird pecking at the iron. Not the door-lion, but the side-passage gate that led to the small yard where we stacked timber.

My father looked up. I rose.

“I will go,” I said.

The yard was a pocket of night, the high walls blotting out the sliver of moon. The air smelled of damp stone and split oak. The gate’s latch was cold under my hand. I opened it.

A figure stood pressed against the outside wall, shrouded in a dark, hooded cloak. For a wild, heart-stopping moment, I thought *Lucas*.

Then she pushed back the hood. Katarina. Her face was pale as a shaving of ivory, her eyes wide and stark.

“You should not be here,” I breathed, pulling her inside and shutting the gate. “If you were seen—”

“I was careful. I used the beggar’s tunnels from the Low Market.” Her voice was breathless, not from running, but from fear. She clutched my arm, her fingers like talons. “Isabeau, you must listen. He knows it’s a decoy.”

The world narrowed to the whites of her eyes. “Arnot?”

“No. The other one. The patron. Thorne.” The name was a curse on her lips. “He has sources inside the Citadel. Someone saw the wood order, the common spruce, the fast-drying glue. He is a man who understands materials. He knows a lute of that size, meant for a master, would not be made from such stock. Not for a man like Arnot to present as a gift.”

The cold that had been pooling in my stomach flooded my veins. “How long has he known?”

“Since the day you accepted the commission.” She swallowed. “He let it proceed. He wanted to see what you would do. What you would hide inside it.”

So the pearl rosette, my beautiful, guilty sacrifice, was a joke. A bauble displayed for an audience already snickering in the dark. The clock I had felt ticking was not counting down to the delivery. It was counting down to my realisation of their contempt.

“Why are you telling me?” I asked, the question brutal in its simplicity.

Tears welled in her eyes, not of sorrow, but of furious shame. “Because I am part of the joke. He told me. Last night. He was… amused. He said you had spirit, but a poor grasp of theatre. He said the best performances are those where the players believe they have a choice in the script.” She looked away, towards the lit window of the workshop. “I am so tired of being amused.”

This was the crack. The fracture line my father had warned of. Not in the glue, but in the conspiracy itself.

“What does he want, Katarina? Truly?”

“The ledger was a key. But the lock has changed. Lucas took something else, or learned something else. Something Thorne did not know was there to be taken. The ledger pointed to greed. This other thing… it points to something Thorne calls ‘the origin of the favour’. To why some men are lifted up and others are ground down. It is a quieter truth, and more dangerous.” She looked back at me, desperate. “They think Lucas told you. They think he passed it to you before he vanished. The decoy lute, the secrecy… it confirms it for them. They are not waiting for the instrument. They are waiting for you to lead them to the real hiding place. Or for you to break, and hand it over.”

The wooden bird in my pocket was an anvil. It was not a mocking echo. It was a lure. Arnot finding the empty cache was a scene staged to make me feel hunted, to make me run to my true secret. They were herding me.

“I don’t have it,” I said, and it was the pure, unvarnished truth.
“Then you are a dead woman,” she said, her voice hollow. “And I am the fool who will have watched it happen.”

A noise from the workshop—a stool scraping. Katarina flinched, her cloak swirling as she pressed back into the shadows. “I cannot come again. He watches me. He calls me his ‘canary in a gilded cage’. He likes to see if I will still sing.”

She pulled up her hood, a phantom dissolving into the deeper dark of the passage. “Do not lead them to your father, Isabeau. That is the one move for which they have no counter. They understand sacrifice. They do not understand a love that refuses to be one.”

She was gone.

I stood in the yard, the night air leaching the warmth from my skin. The controlled discord was gone. I was not the composer of a clever ruse. I was the instrument, and they were plucking the strings, testing for resonance. The decoy was useless. The sacrifice was meaningless. I had only one move left that was truly my own: the truth I did not know.

I returned to the workshop. My father was at his bench, but he was not working. He was sitting with his hands flat on the wood, his head bowed. He had heard.

“You must go,” he said, without lifting his head.

“I will not leave you.”

“You must.” He looked up. His face was old, the lines deep as tool cuts. “They will use me to break you. It is the oldest lever. You have a canny mind, Isabeau, and a brave heart. But you are your mother’s daughter. You will break for love. They know it. So you must remove the lever.”

“Where would I go? They are in the walls, in the Citadel, in the shadows.”

“You go to the one place they have already searched.” He pointed a trembling finger at the loose floorboard. “They found an empty hole. They will not look there again. But you are not looking for what was there. You are looking for *why* it was there. Why that board? Why that spot?” He pushed himself to his feet, a great effort. “Lucas was a troubled boy, but he was not careless. He hid things in layers. A decoy for a decoy. You found his bird. What did he love, before the trouble? Before the debts?”

The question hung in the air, a change of key.

Lucas. Before he was a ghost in taverns, before he was a courier of secrets, he was a boy in this workshop. He was not a maker. He hated the dust, the patience. He loved stories. He loved the ships in the harbour, their names, their destinations. He would carve little toys—poorly, awkwardly—and sell them to sailors for penny tales of Antwerp, of Cadiz, of the Levant. His hiding places were never just hiding places; they were stories.

The wood store. It was where the raw materials lived, the unformed possibilities. He would hide his treasures there, among the potential. The loose board was not near his bed, or in the solar. It was here, in the heart of the thing he resented but understood was our life’s blood.

I walked to the wood store. I did not kneel by the loose board. I looked up. The store was lined with shelves holding blocks and staves, sorted by wood type and age. Above the loose board, at shoulder height, was a row of older, dustier blocks—oak and walnut from my grandfather’s time. I reached up, my fingers brushing over the rough bark edges, the smooth, dry faces.

My fingers found the discrepancy. Not a hidden compartment, but a block that was lighter than it should be. A block of oak, its end grain facing out. I pulled it down. The back was hollowed out, a crude cavity sealed with a thin wafer of wood glued in place. Lucas’s terrible carpentry.

I broke the seal.

Inside was not a ledger. Not a secret document. It was a bundle of cheap paper, covered in my brother’s sprawling, impatient hand. Not accounts. Not secrets.

Stories.

Scribbled tales told to him by sailors. Descriptions of men. Of cargoes. Of deals made on quaysides. Names. Dates. The petty, daily corruptions of a port city. Buried in the middle of a rambling tale about a smuggler named “One-Eyed Jory” was a paragraph that made my breath catch.

*…Jory said the real money wasn’t in the spices or the silk, but in the ‘Lord’s Mercy’. That’s what they call the exemption for ecclesiastical cargo. No duties, no inspections. Jory said Thorne’s man at the customs house, a fellow with a laugh like a saw on nail, stamps the manifest with a special seal—a dove with a broken wing. Jory said they fill the holds with Flemish wool and French wine and Venetian glass, and the manifest says ‘Altar cloths and sacramental wine’. The Lord Provost gets his cut, so he looks the other way. Jory said it’s been running since the old Provost’s time. He said it’s not Thorne’s scheme. Thorne inherited it. He’s just the latest priest at the altar.*

The origin of the favour.

Thorne hadn’t built the machine of corruption. He had been bequeathed it. And Lucas, collecting his sailor’s tales like shiny pebbles, had picked up this one. He hadn’t understood its value until he’d stolen the ledger. The ledger showed the money. This story showed the mechanism, and more importantly, the lineage. It showed that Thorne was not an originator, but a custodian. And custodians can be replaced by those who know where the vault is.

This was the quieter, more dangerous truth. This was what Lucas had truly taken. Not the numbers, but the story behind them. The myth of Thorne’s invulnerability.

But it was just a story. Written on pulp paper, in the hand of a drunken brother. It was no proof. It was a ghost.

I heard my father’s soft intake of breath behind me. He had read it over my shoulder.

“A story is a kind of truth,” he murmured. “But it needs a witness to give it weight.”

“One-Eyed Jory is likely dead or halfway to the Indies,” I said, the hope that had flared dying back to embers.

“Not Jory,” my father said. He pointed a gnarled finger at the last line of the entry. “The man with the laugh like a saw on nail. The customs man with the seal.”

He was right. The story named no names, but it described a man in a specific place, with a specific, grotesque duty. A man who might be persuaded to bear witness, if the pressure was applied to the right point.

The hook was no longer set for me. I held, in my hands, a hook of my own making. A crude, ugly thing, carved from hearsay and suspicion. But a hook nonetheless.

I looked from the papers to the decoy lute, gleaming emptily in the lamplight. Its beautiful, fraudulent rosette seemed to wink. A decoy for a decoy. A story for a ledger. They were waiting for me to lead them to a secret. I would lead them to a man.

But first, I had to find him. And I had to do it before Arnot, or Thorne, realised the empty cache had not been empty at all. It had held the one thing they never considered: a brother’s memory, poorly carved, but deeply hidden.

I placed the oak block back on its shelf. I folded the papers and slipped them into my apron, beside the wooden bird. The two weights balanced each other.

“I will need to visit the customs house,” I said, my voice strange in the quiet workshop. “To consult on the wood for the lute’s travel case. A matter of humidity during transport.”

My father nodded, understanding the new script. “A prudent craftsman considers every variable.”

“It will be dangerous.”
“It already is,” he said. He reached out and touched my cheek, a rare, dry gesture. “Play this next part in a clear tone, daughter. No discord. The most dangerous note is the one everyone expects, played perfectly on time.”

He turned and walked slowly up the stairs to the solar, leaving me alone with the instruments and the ticking of the stove.

I looked at the decoy lute, my beautiful, useless sacrifice. It was no longer a clock. It was a prop. And the performance was entering its final act. The stage was set not in the Lord Provost’s mansion, but in the damp, bureaucratic bowels of the customs house, a place of stamps and seals and lies told in triplicate.

I had my hook. Now I needed to find the fish. And I had to do it while the men in the wings still believed I was singing the song they had written for me.



---

# Chapter 17

## **CHAPTER 17**

The customs house was a fortress of ledgers. It squatted at the head of the broad, filthy quay, a block of soot-stained sandstone built to repel joy. Its windows were narrow, grudging slits. The air around it did not smell of salt and fish, but of damp paper, iron-gall ink, and the sour tang of men who spent their lives quantifying the world into columns. It was the antithesis of my father’s workshop. Here, nothing was shaped or sung into being. Things were only recorded, taxed, and permitted to pass.

My pretext was thin, but legitimate. The Lord Provost’s lute required a travel case. Spanish cedar was best for such a thing, resistant to ship-damp and worm. A shipment had arrived last week from the Baltic. I needed to inspect the wood, to judge its quality and its price. A craftsman’s due diligence.

The guard at the heavy oak door was a man whose face had settled into permanent suspicion. He examined my guild token as if it might be counterfeit, his eyes flicking from the engraved lyre to my face.

“Wood inspection,” he grunted, the words a verdict of profound irrelevance. “Archives and manifests, second floor. Clerk Dawes.”

The interior was a cavern of bureaucratic murmur. High ceilings swallowed sound, turning the clatter of stamping presses and the low drone of clerks into a monotonous hymn. Rows of tall desks ran the length of the main hall, each manned by a bent figure scribbling in triplicate. The walls were lined not with instruments, but with shelves straining under the weight of bound ledgers, their spines marked with years and ports of entry. *Antwerp. Bergen. Bordeaux. Danzig.*

I climbed the stone stairs, my boots echoing in a way that felt accusatory. The second floor was a labyrinth of smaller rooms, their doors open to reveal more shelves, more clerks, more of the ceaseless, quiet industry of control. I found a door marked *Manifests – Baltic & Northern Trades*. Inside, a young man with ink-stained cuffs and a tired slope to his shoulders looked up from a massive, spread-open ledger.

“Clerk Dawes?”

“I am he.” His voice was softer than I expected, almost drowned by the paper around him.

I presented my token again, recited my purpose. He listened, nodding slowly, his eyes on the token rather than me. “The *Fortuna’s* cargo. Yes. The cedar is in the bonded warehouse, quay three. The ledger entry is…” He turned pages with a practiced flip, his finger tracing down a column. “Here. Lot seventeen. Twenty planks, Spanish cedar, consigned to… ah.”

He stopped. His finger did not move from the line. He looked up at me, and the tiredness in his face sharpened into something else. A flicker of recognition that had nothing to do with my guild token.

“Consigned to?” I prompted, my voice steady. A clear tone.

“Van der Hagen,” he said, too quietly. “The consignee is listed as M. van der Hagen.”

My father. A routine order, placed months ago. Utterly normal. And yet the way Clerk Dawes said it, the way his eyes darted to the open door and back, made the name sound like a secret.

“I see,” I said. “May I examine the manifest? For the… bill of lading details. The workshop’s records must be precise.”

He hesitated. A clerk’s hesitation was a shout in this place of procedure. “Of course,” he said finally. He pulled a different, slimmer ledger from a shelf, opened it to a marked page. “The full cargo list for the *Fortuna*.”

I stepped closer, under the pretext of studying the spidery script. The cedar entry was innocuous. But my gaze drifted up the page, down. I was not looking for wood. I was looking for a name. For a pattern. For the ghost of a transaction that would not be recorded in any official column.

“A large shipment,” I murmured, my finger trailing down the list. Iron ingots. Rye. Fustian cloth. Tar.

“A profitable one,” Dawes agreed, his voice still low. “For some.”

The hook in my apron pocket seemed to grow heavier. The crude wooden fish. Lucas had loved whittling such things, his knife biting into soft pine, creating clumsy, cheerful shapes. *A brother’s memory, poorly carved, but deeply hidden.*

I looked at Dawes. He was not looking at the ledger. He was looking at my hands, at the faint, permanent stain of varnish on my fingertips. A craftsman’s mark.

“My brother,” I said, the two words dropping into the quiet room like stones into a still pool. “Lucas van der Hagen. He had a fondness for the quays. For stories. I wonder… did he ever come here? To ask about shipments? About stories from abroad?”

Dawes’s breath caught. It was a tiny sound, but in the papery silence, it was a crack. He closed the ledger slowly, carefully, as if it were made of glass.

“This is a house of records, Mistress van der Hagen. Not of stories.”

“Records tell their own stories,” I said. “If one knows how to read the spaces between the lines.”

He stood very still. Then he walked to the door and closed it. The click of the latch was final. When he turned back, his face was pale, but his eyes were clear.

“Your brother,” he said, “asked about spaces between lines. He was a danger to himself. He asked about manifests that did not balance. About tariffs paid on goods that never appeared in the bonded warehouses. He asked about the *White Hart*.”

The name hung in the air. It was not a ship on the ledger before me. It was a ghost ship.

“The *White Hart*,” I repeated.

“A regular trader. Baltic routes. Its manifests were always in perfect order. Too perfect.” Dawes’s words came faster now, a quiet, desperate spill. “Lucas thought the *Hart* was a shadow. That its true cargo was never logged. He believed it was used to move… other things. People. Information. Payment. He came three times. The last time, he was not asking. He was telling. He said he had a name. The man who orchestrated the shadows. The man here, in the customs house, who made the imperfections vanish.”

My heart was a hard, cold thing in my chest. “What name?”

Dawes shook his head, a frantic motion. “He did not say. He said he needed proof. Something more than a whisper. He said he would find it in the *Hart*’s private berth log. Not the official one. The captain’s private copy. It was kept here, he was sure of it. In the Surveyor of Customs’ personal archive.”

“And did he find it?”

“I do not know. He never came back.” Dawes’s gaze was direct now, full of a fearful pity. “The day after he spoke of the private log, the *White Hart*’s records were flagged for a special audit. A routine matter, it was said. The relevant ledgers and logs were moved to the Surveyor’s office for review. They have not returned to the public archive.”

The hook twisted. Lucas had been close. He had seen the shape of the mechanism—not just a smuggled lute or a bribed official, but a system. A ship that was a ledger entry, a ghost that moved real things. And he had stepped into the mechanism’s path.

“The Surveyor of Customs,” I said. “Who is he?”

Dawes almost smiled, a bitter, thin expression. “Master Alistair Thorne.”

The world did not tilt. It snapped into a configuration of terrible, crystalline clarity. Thorne. The Provost’s man. The patron who had commissioned the decoy lute. The man with the calm voice and the eyes that inventoried souls. He was not just a courtier facilitating a theft. He *was* the system. The customs house was his domain. The *White Hart* was his instrument. He did not need to steal the Silver Lute. He needed to control its journey, its narrative, its very existence in the ledgers of the world. He was the author of the blank spaces.

And my brother had tried to read his manuscript.

“The private berth log,” I said. “It would still be in his office?”

“If it exists. If it was not… lost in the audit.” Dawes’s meaning was clear. *Destroyed.* “His office is on the third floor. The west corner. It is always locked.”

I looked at the closed door, then back at the terrified clerk. He had given me everything he had. A name. A location. A ghost ship. It was more than I had come for. It was a map to the heart of the trap.

“You have been very helpful, Clerk Dawes.”

“I have been a fool,” he whispered. “They will know I have spoken to you. Your visit will be noted.”

“Then note this as well,” I said, pulling my guild token from my pocket and placing it deliberately on his desk. “A craftswoman inspecting wood. A routine query. You showed her the ledger. She left. Nothing more.”

I saw the hope and the terror war in his eyes. He gave a stiff nod.

I left the room, walking back into the murmur of the main hall. My steps were measured, my face a mask of mild frustration, a woman whose business had been concluded. But my mind was racing through the architecture of the building, the location of the west corner, the pattern of the guards.

I descended to the main floor and made for the large double doors. As I passed a particularly high desk, a man stepped into my path. He was not a guard. He wore the dark, fine wool of a senior clerk, but his posture was all soldier. Arnot’s man. He had a face like a hatchet, and his eyes were the same flat grey as the North Sea in winter.

“Mistress van der Hagen,” he said. His voice was a neutral thing, devoid of the clerk’s drone. “Your inspection is complete?”

“It is,” I said, not breaking stride. “The wood is satisfactory. I will inform my father.”

“Master Thorne was informed of your visit. He wishes to ensure you found all you required.” The man fell into step beside me, a shadow in broad daylight.

“That is kind. Please convey my thanks. The resources here are… comprehensive.”

We reached the doors. The guard opened one, and the quay’s damp, raucous air rushed in. The hatchet-faced man stopped at the threshold, his domain ending at the stone step.

“He also wished me to remind you,” the man said, his grey eyes holding mine, “of the lute’s imminent journey. The case must be ready. All variables accounted for. A prudent craftsman considers every detail.”

It was my father’s phrase, repeated back to me with the precision of a threat. They were watching. They had been watching. My visit was expected, perhaps even scripted. But they did not know about Clerk Dawes. They did not know about the hook in my pocket, now pointing directly at their master.

“Every detail,” I agreed. I stepped out onto the quay.

The door closed behind me, shutting off the world of ledgers and lies. I stood for a moment amidst the barrels and the shouting stevedores, the gulls wheeling overhead. The customs house loomed at my back, a cliff of secrets. Thorne was in there, in his west corner office. And somewhere within those walls, perhaps in a locked drawer or a burnt-out hearth, was the last thing my brother had sought. The private log of the *White Hart*.

I had come to find a fish. I had found the fisherman.

But a hook is useless if the line is cut. I needed that log. I needed the proof Lucas had died for. Thorne had moved it, hidden it, or destroyed it. To walk back into the workshop and simply wait for his next move was to play the note everyone expected. It was to surrender.

I began to walk, not towards the house of the Red Door, but away from it, into the tangled, reeking wynds that fed into the High Street. The performance was entering its final act, but the stage had shifted again. It was no longer the customs house. It was Thorne’s office. And I had no script, no patron, no decoy.

Only a key.

The thought arrived fully formed, cold and sharp. The Lord Provost’s mansion. Thorne had an office there, too. A place of polished wood and quiet power. But he was here, in his fortress of ledgers. His other domain would be quieter, guarded by household servants, not by hatchet-faced men. The Provost was a prisoner in his own rooms, surrounded by Thorne’s influence. But the household still ran. There were still routines, gaps, shadows.

And I had been inside. I had seen the corridors, the anterooms. I had delivered the decoy lute.

I had also, in a moment of desperate improvisation weeks ago, taken something. A small, heavy iron key from a ring in Thorne’s own anteroom. A key of unknown purpose, stolen as potential leverage, now burning a hole in the secret pouch sewn into my skirt. I had almost forgotten it, this tiny, cold tooth of metal.

I did not know what lock it fit. But I knew its twin would be in the customs house. It was a key to Thorne’s world. And one half of that world was vulnerable.

The hook was set. The fish was named. And I held, not a gaff to land him, but a key to his own house.

I turned my steps towards the climb to the High Street, towards the looming silhouette of the Lord Provost’s mansion on its ridge. The decoy lute was there, a beautiful, silent witness. It was time to give it a new role. It was no longer a prop. It was a distraction.

I would return to the workshop. I would tell my father the wood was satisfactory. I would work on the travel case. I would play my part in perfect, expected time.

And when the curtain of night fell, I would slip from the house of the Red Door not as a luthier, but as a thief in my own city. I would use my stolen key to pick the lock on the other half of Thorne’s life. I would search for a ghost in a ledger, for my brother’s final proof.

The most dangerous note, my father said, is the one everyone expects, played perfectly on time.

Tonight, I would play a rest. A silence. And in that silence, I would turn the key.



---

# Chapter 18

## **CHAPTER 18**

The workshop was a sepulchre of good intentions. My father’s voice, reading from Boethius, was a steady drone from the solar, a sound that had always meant safety and now felt like the turning of a key in a lock behind me. I stood in the centre of the room, the stolen iron key a cold, familiar weight against my thigh, and told him the lie.

“The spruce is satisfactory,” I said, my voice cutting through the murmur from above. “A fine, tight grain. It will do.”

The reading paused. I could picture him, the heavy book open on his knees, his spectacles perched on the end of his nose. “Good,” he said, the word a soft exhalation of relief. “The wood chooses the instrument, Isabeau. It is a partnership. You must listen.”

*I am listening*, I thought. *I am listening to the silence where my brother’s voice should be. I am listening to the click of a lock that has not yet turned.* “I will begin the travel case,” I said.

“The client expects delivery tomorrow evening. The timing is… delicate.”

“It will be ready.”

The reading resumed. I went to the bench where the decoy lute lay, its body a hollow shell of exquisite craftsmanship. My fingers traced the curve of its ribs, the precise inlay at the rosette. A beautiful lie. I had built it to be seen, to be admired, to be a vessel for a story Thorne would believe. Now I needed it to be invisible.

I worked. The scrape of my plane against the oak for the case was a rhythmic, innocent sound. I measured, I joined, I smoothed. My hands performed their familiar liturgy while my mind paced the corridors of the Lord Provost’s mansion. I saw the polished floorboards, the sombre portraits, the door to Thorne’s anteroom. I felt the weight of the key in my palm, its teeth a silent question.

The day bled away, the light in the workshop thickening to gold, then to grey. I finished the case, lined it with green baize, and nestled the lute inside. The fit was perfect. A completed task. A closed circle.

My father descended for supper. We ate salt fish and boiled greens in a silence that was its own kind of conversation. He spoke of glue temperatures, of a tricky repair on a viol bow. I answered in monosyllables, my mind a room elsewhere. He looked at me, his eyes clouded with a concern he could not name. “You are quiet tonight, Isabeau.”

“The case was fiddly work. It requires concentration.”

He nodded, accepting the offering. “Concentration is a form of prayer,” he said, echoing his own master. “But even prayers must end. The mind needs rest.”

“Yes,” I said. “It does.”

I cleared the plates. I banked the fire. I performed the evening rites of the house of the Red Door, each movement a stitch in the fabric of a normal night. When my father retired to the solar, his footsteps heavy on the stairs, I stood alone in the darkening workshop.

I did not change my clothes. To change would be to admit I was becoming something else. I was still Isabeau van der Hagen, luthier. I simply had an errand to run. A key to return. A truth to find.

I slipped out through the side door, the one that led to the narrow close. The Haar was coming in again, a cold, wet breath from the sea, swallowing the sounds of the city. The cobbles were slick underfoot. I became a shadow among shadows, climbing the steep, twisting wynds from our house in the Luckenbooths towards the High Street.

The Lord Provost’s mansion loomed on its ridge, a darker bulk against the dark sky. Its windows were mostly shuttered, a few glowing with the soft, expensive light of oil lamps. Not the harsh tallow of the customs house. This was a place of subdued wealth, of power worn smooth by generations.

I did not approach the main entrance. I skirted the high wall, my memory supplying the layout I had observed weeks before. The delivery entrance. A smaller, arched door of stout oak, set back in a recess. I had used it before. A servant, harried and suspicious, had taken the decoy lute from me. I had noted the lock then. A good one, but not exceptional.

My tools were not those of a burglar, but of a luthier: a set of fine, strong needles I used for soundhole purfling, a length of stiff steel wire, a sliver of whalebone. And the key.

I worked quickly, my back to the close, the fog my ally. The lock yielded not with a thief’s cunning, but with a craftsman’s understanding of tension and alignment. It clicked open with a sound like a falling coin.

I was inside. A stone-flagged passage, smelling of damp wool and onions. The kitchens. Silence. The household was abed, or attending the Provost in his rooms. I moved, a ghost in a house of ghosts, my heart a frantic bird against my ribs.

I found the main corridor. My footsteps were swallowed by a long, runner carpet. Portraits of grim-faced men in ruffs watched my progress. I knew the way. To the left, the grand staircase leading to the Provost’s private apartments. To the right, a more modest hallway. Thorne’s territory.

His anteroom door was before me. A plain, dark door. I put my ear to the wood. Nothing. I tried the handle. Locked.

This was the moment. The iron key was slick in my sweating palm. I fitted it to the lock. It was not a perfect match. The teeth were different. My breath caught. But I turned it, feeling the mechanism resist, then accept. The lock gave with a solid, satisfying *thunk*.

I was in.

The room was as I remembered: a desk, two chairs, a cabinet for papers. Neat, austere, impersonal. It did not smell of him, of that particular blend of ink and aniseed. This was a stage set. An office for show.

I went to the desk. The drawers were locked, but these were simpler mechanisms. My purfling needle found the wards. The first drawer held correspondence, bland and official. The second, inventories of household goods. The third was empty.

A cold dread began to pool in my stomach. This was a dead end. A clean, meaningless room.

Then I looked at the cabinet. It was a fine piece, oak with brass fittings. The keyhole was larger, older. On impulse, I tried the iron key again. It slid in. It turned. The door swung open.

Inside were not papers. It was a small, private armoury. A pair of elegant, deadly wheellock pistols nestled in velvet. A thin, wicked stiletto. And ledgers. Three of them, bound in plain calfskin, identical to the ones in the customs house fortress.

I lifted one out. It was heavier than it should be. I opened it.

These were not records of tariffs or ship manifests. The columns were names, dates, amounts. But the names were not merchants. They were councillors, bailiffs, ministers, guild masters. The amounts were not in pounds sterling, but in crowns, ducats, livres. Foreign currencies. Payments. Next to each entry, a tiny, precise notation in Thorne’s hand: *Favour, Silence, Vote, Recantation.*

This was the ledger of influence. The real currency of his power.

My hands trembled as I turned the pages. I was looking for a ghost. For Lucas. I found him not in the payments, but in the margins.

Halfway through the second book, a name was circled, not entered in a column. *Van der Hagen, L.* And below it, a single phrase: **Ear of the Provost. Music room. Resolved.**

*Resolved.*

The word was a physical blow. I gripped the edge of the cabinet. The fog outside seemed to seep into my bones.

Then, a sound. A soft *click* from the corridor. Not a lock. A latch.

I froze, the ledger still open in my hands. The door to the anteroom swung inward.

It was not a guard. It was the Lord Provost himself.

He stood in the doorway, a frail man wrapped in a brocade dressing gown, his face pale and drawn in the lamplight from the hall. He did not look surprised. He looked exhausted. And behind him, a step back in the shadow, was a woman I recognized—Mistress Drummond, the Provost’s sharp-eyed housekeeper, the one who had accepted the lute.

“You found them, then,” the Provost said, his voice a dry rustle. He did not sound angry. He sounded relieved.

I could not speak. I held the ledger out, a mute accusation.

He stepped into the room, closing the door softly behind him, leaving Mistress Drummond outside. He glanced at the open cabinet, at the ledger in my hands. “He told me you would come. He said you were tenacious. Like your brother.”

“Where is he?” The words were torn from me, raw and loud in the quiet room.

The Provost sank into one of the chairs, a man defeated by more than illness. “You read the word. *Resolved*. Thorne is a man of clean lines. He does not like loose ends.”

“Is he dead?” The question hung in the air, monstrous.

“I do not know,” the Provost whispered. “I only know that he is gone. And that his… association with my household was a source of great embarrassment. A young luthier, coming and going at odd hours. Whispers. Thorne offered to resolve it. I allowed it.” He looked at me, his eyes pleading for an understanding I could not give. “He controls the port. The city’s wealth. He controls the votes on my council. What is one boy’s freedom, or one girl’s grief, against the stability of an entire city?”

The false victory of the past days shattered inside me. The plan, the key, the clever infiltration—it was all a child’s game. I had been chasing evidence, while Thorne had been wielding the blunt instrument of consequence. I had thought I was playing a rest, a strategic silence. But the silence I had entered was not mine. It was Lucas’s. It was the silence of *resolved*.

“He knew I would come here,” I said, the truth dawning with icy clarity. “The key. It was too easy. He knew I had it. He let me keep it. This room… it’s a trap. But not for me.”

“For me,” the Provost said. “You are proof. A burglar in my house, finding his private records. If this comes to light, my weakness is exposed. My complicity. He owns me completely now.” He put his face in his hands. “I am a prisoner in silk sheets.”

The hook was not set by me. I was the hook, and Thorne had used me to set it deeper into the Provost’s flesh. My rebellion had been scripted. My freedom was a constraint he had designed.

I heard footsteps then, firm and measured, coming down the corridor. Not the soft shuffle of the Provost. These were the steps of a man who owned the ground he walked on.

The door opened.

Reynard Thorne stood on the threshold. He was not in his customs house grey, but in a dark, finely-cut coat. He looked from the Provost, crumpled in the chair, to me standing by the open cabinet, the ledger in my hand. His expression was one of mild, profound disappointment.

“Isabeau,” he said. “The lute case you delivered is exquisite. A masterpiece of its kind. But I fear you have mistaken its purpose. It was not meant to carry an instrument. It was meant to close a case.”

He stepped inside. Mistress Drummond’s face, pale and grim, appeared behind him before she pulled the door shut. We were locked in with him.

“My lord Provost,” Thorne said, his voice gentle. “You look unwell. You should not be out of your bed.” It was not concern. It was a command.

The Provost flinched.

Thorne’s gaze settled on me. “You have a talent for being where you should not be. First my customs house. Now my private office. You seek answers with the subtlety of a hammer.”

“What did you do to him?” My voice was a thread.

“I resolved a problem,” Thorne said. He walked to the cabinet, took the ledger from my numb fingers, and closed it with a soft thud. “Your brother made a choice. He involved himself in affairs beyond his station. He used his access to this house, to the Provost’s fondness for music, to eavesdrop on conversations, to pass information to certain… discontented elements. He was a leak. I plug leaks.”

“Where is he?”

“Gone. That is all you need to know. It is more than most get.” He placed the ledger back in the cabinet, locked it, and pocketed the key. My key was still in the lock, a useless piece of metal. “The tragedy is your persistence. You had a path. A good, honest trade. You could have grieved in private and carried on. Now…” He sighed, a man burdened by the foolishness of others. “Now you have broken into the Lord Provost’s mansion. You have stolen his private correspondence. You have assaulted his person.”

“I have done no such thing!” The Provost’s protest was a weak gasp.

“You are distressed, my lord. Confused. Your illness affects your mind. Mistress Drummond will testify to the state she found you in. And I have a burglar, caught red-handed, with a housebreaker’s tools.” He nodded at my purfling needle and wire, still on the desk. “The law is clear.”

The law was his. The city was his. The story was his to write.

This was the deeper truth I had missed. The constraint was not a locked door or a guarded ledger. It was the narrative. He controlled the story. I was the villain, the mad girl, the thief. Lucas was the resolved problem. The Provost was the feeble victim. Thorne was the protector, the restorer of order.

My freedom had been an illusion. A space he had allowed me to roam, knowing every path led back to his hand.

“What do you want?” The question was ash in my mouth.

“For you to understand the score,” he said. “The music is written. You can play your assigned part, or you can create a discord that will be silenced. Permanently. Your father’s workshop operates on my sufferance. His commissions come through my port. His safety exists in my shadow. That is the contract you have broken.”

He walked to the door, opened it. Mistress Drummond and a large, silent footman I recognized from the customs house stood outside. “Escort Miss van der Hagen home,” Thorne said. “See her to her door. Ensure she causes no further disturbance tonight.”

It was not a kindness. It was a demonstration. He could have thrown me in the Tolbooth. He could have ended it. Instead, he was sending me home, to my father, to the workshop. He was showing me the walls of my cage. He was making me live in the silence I had tried to fight.

The footman took my arm, his grip firm. I was led away, past the Provost, who would not meet my eyes, down the grand corridor, through the kitchens, and out into the swallowing fog.

The walk back to the house of the Red Door was a funeral procession. The footman released me at our door-lion. He did not speak. He simply stood, a solid shadow, until I went inside.

The workshop was dark. The air still smelled of wood and varnish. The decoy lute in its perfect case sat on the bench, a monument to my folly.

I climbed the stairs to my small room. I did not weep. The shock was too complete.

I had thought I was turning a key. But the key had turned me. I had walked into the silence, and the silence had a name, and a face, and it owned everything, even my grief.

From my window, I watched the footman’s shadow merge with the fog and disappear. He was gone. But the constraint remained. It was in the air. It was in the law. It was in the word *resolved*.

The hook for the next move was not mine to set. It was mine to feel, buried deep, drawing me towards a choice I did not want to make: to play the rest forever, in perfect, expected time, or to become a discord so profound it would break the instrument itself.

The most dangerous note, my father said, is the one everyone expects.

But there is another kind of danger. It is the note that is never played at all. The string that is cut.

And in the dark, I began to wonder what sound it would make.



---

# Chapter 19

## **CHAPTER 19**

The silence after the footman dissolved into the fog was a new substance. It was not the quiet of the workshop at night, which was a patient, listening thing. It was the silence of a verdict. It filled the house of the Red Door like poured lead, sealing every crack, weighting every breath. I stood in the dark of the shop, my hand on the cold, carved mane of the door-lion, and understood the geometry of a cage. Its bars were not iron, but permission. Its lock was not a mechanism, but a word: *resolved*.

I climbed the stairs. Past my father’s closed door, behind which I heard no reading, no movement—only a deeper, more private silence. Into my own room. I did not light a candle. The fog pressed a grey, woolen light against the window, enough to see the shapes of things: the bed, the stool, the case of my own lute leaning in the corner. The instrument I had not played for weeks. The music I had let go quiet.

I sat on the edge of the bed, my bones heavy with a fatigue that had nothing to do with sleep. Thorne’s study replayed behind my eyes, not as a memory, but as a diagram. The Provost’s averted gaze. The footman’s impersonal grip. Thorne’s voice, cool and final, laying the truth out like a merchant displaying spoiled goods. *The matter is resolved.* My evidence was nothing. My accusation was air. Lucas was a closed ledger, a balance struck in blood and gold and bureaucratic ink.

The choice he had outlined was an elegant torture. Play the expected part. Be the luthier’s daughter, the quiet woman in the quiet room, making beautiful, compliant sounds for a beautiful, compliant world. Or become a discord so profound it would mean my own destruction. To break the instrument—myself, the shop, my father’s fragile peace—in the process.

For three days, I moved through the house as a ghost moves through a familiar haunt. I performed the rituals. I swept the workshop floor, the fragrant cedar and pine shavings curling around the broom. I helped my father glue a new rose into a gittern’s soundhole, my hands steady, his instructions minimal. We ate our meals in the solar, the silence between us a third, uninvited guest. The decoy lute in its perfect case remained on the main bench. Neither of us touched it. It was a headstone.

My father watched me. I felt his gaze like a soft, constant pressure on the back of my neck. He was waiting for the storm to break. For the tears, the rage, the shattered thing he would have to try and mend. But the storm did not come. The silence had frozen it inside me. I was a vessel full of ice.

On the fourth morning, a different kind of letter arrived.

It was not jammed in the door-lion, but delivered properly, by a sharp-faced boy in the livery of the Kirkwall carriers. A heavy, cream-laid paper, sealed not with wax but with a small, crisp wafer. My father took it from the boy, his brows drawing together as he read the inscription.

“For you,” he said, holding it out. His voice was cautious.

The paper was cool and expensive under my fingers. The script was a clean, vertical copperplate. It bore no name, only an address and a time.

*The Custom House Wharf. The warehouse marked with three white bars. One hour after evening bell.*

That was all.

I turned it over. Nothing. The anonymity was absolute, and therefore deliberate. It was not a summons from Thorne; he had no need for secrecy. It was not from anyone in the Provost’s circle. This was something else. A wrong note in the severe harmony of my new constraint.

“Who is it from?” my father asked.

“I don’t know.”

“You will not go.”

It was not a question. I looked at him. The worry in his eyes was a live thing, fluttering against the cage of his composure. He had seen me led away by Thorne’s man. He knew the shape of the trouble, even if he did not know its full name.

“It may be about Lucas,” I said, the words ash in my mouth.

“Nothing good waits for you in warehouses after dark, Isabeau. Lucas is gone. This… this is a hook. You will swallow it, and it will drag you into deeper water.” He reached for my hand, his own scarred and resin-stained. “Please. Let the silence be. It is the only safety left.”

His touch was warm. It was the warmth of the workshop, of known things, of a life measured in frets and scales. It was the warmth of the cage. For a moment, I wanted to collapse into it. To let the decision be made for me. To play the expected note forever.

But I thought of the other silence. The one in Thorne’s study. The one that owned my brother’s memory. That silence was not safety. It was a burial shroud.

“I have to know,” I said, and my voice sounded strange to me, hollow and certain.

I went. Not for hope, but for the opposite. To meet the finality of it, whatever it was. To prove to myself that the cage had no doors, only well-appointed walls.

I wore a dark, plain cloak. I left by the back lane, a route of dripping walls and refuse heaps. The evening bell had already tolled, its sound swallowed by the Haar, which had returned, thicker than ever. The fog was a collaborator, wrapping the world in a muffling wool. It erased distances, softened edges, turned familiar wynds into anonymous tunnels. It was the perfect medium for secrets.

The Custom House Wharf was a teeth-line of stone and timber jutting into the unseen water. Sounds were distorted: the groan of a distant ship, the clank of a chain, a low shout that seemed to come from everywhere and nowhere. The warehouses loomed like sleeping beasts, their broad doors shut, their signs obscured.

I found the one with three white bars painted roughly on its side door. The paint was fresh, stark against the weathered wood. A signal.

I stood before the door, my heart a slow, heavy drum. This was the precipice. To turn back was to accept the constraint. To go forward was to risk the discord. I thought of the string, cut. What sound would it make?

I lifted the latch. The door opened without a sound, well-oiled.

Inside, the darkness was not complete. High, grimy windows allowed ghosts of fog-light to fall in slanting columns. The space was vast, cavernous, smelling of damp hemp, old grain, and the cold, mineral scent of the sea. Crates and barrels were stacked in vague, hulking formations. The air was still, dead.

“Close the door.”

The voice came from my right, low and rough. A woman’s voice.

I pushed the door shut. The scrape of the latch was obscenely loud in the stillness.

“Here.”

I followed the sound around a stack of timber baulks. A small space had been cleared, lit by a single, shuttered lantern set on a barrel. By its frugal light, I saw her.

She was perhaps fifty, but life had carved its passage deeply. Her face was lean, weathered, her hair a thick, grey braid over one shoulder. She wore the practical, tar-stained trousers and thick jersey of a ship’s crew, not the skirts of the town. Her eyes, as she assessed me, were the colour of flint, and just as sharp. I knew her, and yet I did not.

“Mistress Drummond,” I said, the pieces slotting together with a quiet, shocking click.

Thorne’s housekeeper. The silent, efficient woman who had brought the footman to the study door. Who moved through his house like a well-trained shadow.

A faint, grim smile touched her mouth. “Aye. Though not in this place. In this place, you’ll call me Elspeth.”

“You work for him.”

“I keep his house. There’s a difference.” Her gaze was unflinching. “I knew your brother. Lucas.”

The sound of his name in this place, from this woman, was a physical blow. The ice inside me cracked. “How?”

She leaned back against a crate, folding her arms. The gesture was not defensive, but settled, as if beginning a long-awaited tale. “He did work for Thorne, as you guessed. Fetching, carrying. Listening. But he was a bad liar, your brother. His face was an open book. Thorne used that. Made him a decoy, a flash of colour to draw the eye while other, quieter hands did the real work.”

“The real work.”

“Thorne’s business is control,” she said, her voice flat. “Control of the trade through the port. Control of the men on the council. Control of the stories people tell. Your brother saw a ledger he shouldn’t have. Not just numbers. Names. Payments to the garrison commander to look the other way when certain ships came in. Bribes to the gauger to undervalue cargo. Fees paid to a particular Edinburgh magistrate to ensure favourable rulings.”

She paused, letting the shape of it form in the damp, dark air. A web, not a single crime.

“Lucas was frightened. He had a conscience, that boy. A fatal thing in that house. He came to me. Thought, because I was a woman, because I seemed kind to him, I might be an ally. He asked me what he should do.”

The warehouse seemed to grow colder. “What did you tell him?”

“I told him to run.” Her flint eyes held mine. “I gave him what coin I could spare. Told him of a coaster, the *Marlin*, making for Rotterdam the next tide. A clean ship, not one of Thorne’s. He was to be on it.”

“But he wasn’t.”

“No.” The word was final. “He was afraid for you. For your father. He thought if he just took the ledger, had proof, he could force Thorne to leave you all be. A child’s notion. He went back to the study that night. Thorne was waiting for him.”

I saw it then. Not a robbery. An execution. Lucas, cornered in that room of books and power, the ledger in his hands. Thorne, calm, inevitable. The Provost, perhaps, called in to witness the *resolution*.

“The Provost said it was a footpad. A random crime.”

Elspeth’s smile was a thin, bitter cut. “The Provost’s investments are tied to Thorne’s shipping ventures. His silence was purchased with your brother’s blood. The footpad was a drunk lad from the docks, paid to take the blame, to be found with Lucas’s purse. He hanged for it last month. Neat. Tidy. *Resolved*.”

The silence that followed was different from the one in my room. This one was alive, humming with the terrible truth. It was not a constraint, but a charge. I found I could breathe it.

“Why tell me this?” My voice was a whisper. “You could have kept your silence. You are safe in his house.”

“Safe?” She barked a short, harsh laugh. “I am a piece of furniture to him. Useful, invisible. But I am not a stone. I have carried your brother’s fear for a year. I have watched Thorne grow fatter on his control. And I watched you walk into his study with fire in your eyes. I watched him extinguish it. He enjoys that, you see. The snuffing out. It is his greatest pleasure.”

She pushed away from the crate and stepped closer. The lantern light carved deep lines in her face. “He thinks he has snuffed you out. He thinks you will go back to your workshop and make your pretty music and be a good, silent girl. That is his mistake. He does not understand that some silences are combustible.”

She reached into her jersey and pulled out a small, oilskin packet, tied with cord. “Lucas did not get the ledger. But he was clever. He made a copy. Key pages, key names, amounts. He hid it in the one place he thought no one would look: the lining of an old cloak in my sewing room. He gave it to me for safekeeping the day before he died. Said if anything happened to him, I should get it to someone who could use it.”

She held out the packet. It seemed to suck the light from the air around it.

“I am not a hero, Isabeau. I am a housekeeper. I cannot take this to the magistrates in Edinburgh. They would ask how I came by it. Thorne would hear of it before the ink was dry on my statement. I would end up in the Forth with a stone round my neck.” She thrust it toward me. “But you… you are already in the fire. You have nothing left to lose but the cage. And you have a reason to make a sound so discordant it could crack his world.”

I looked at the packet. It was not large. It weighed nothing. It weighed everything. It was the key that had turned in the lock, now offered to my hand.

“What would you have me do with it?”

“That is your choice. Burn it. Go home. The silence will be comfortable, in time. Or take it. Find a way to get it to someone beyond Thorne’s reach. Someone with the power and the will to use it. It will be dangerous. It will likely get you killed.” Her eyes were relentless. “But it is the only note left that matters. The one he does not expect.”

I thought of my father’s hand, warm on mine. *Let the silence be.* I thought of the decoy lute, perfect and mute on the bench. I thought of Lucas, his open face, trying to be brave.

I took the packet.

The oilskin was cool, slightly rough. I slipped it inside my dress, against my skin. It felt like a second heart, a secret, furious pulse.

Elspeth nodded, once. A transaction was complete. “There is a man,” she said, her voice dropping even lower. “A writer. In Edinburgh. He prints pamphlets that annoy the powerful. He has tried to write about Thorne before, but had no proof, only rumour. He is not afraid. His name is Gideon Lockhart. He keeps a print-shop in the Luckenbooths.”

“How do I get this to him? Thorne will be watching me. The footman…”

“The footman watches the front. There are other ways out of a city. And other ways to travel than by the main roads.” She studied me. “Can you ride?”

“A little. Long ago.”

“It will have to be enough. There is a carrier, an honest man. He leaves from the Grassmarket at dawn tomorrow with a train of pack-ponies for Linlithgow. From there, you can find your way to Edinburgh. It is two days of hard riding. You will be missed. Thorne will suspect. He may come for your father.”

A new coldness, sharper than the fog, pierced me. “I cannot leave him.”
