“You must. It is the only chance for either of you. If you stay, you are both prisoners. If you go, and succeed, you break the lock. If you go and fail…” She shrugged. “Your father is a respected craftsman. Thorne has no public quarrel with him. His usefulness as a hostage ends if you are gone. He will be watched, but he should be safe. Safer than if you stay and plot in his attic.”

The logic was brutal, and sound. It was the logic of war, not of home.

“I will need to tell him something.”

“Tell him you are going to find your music again. It is not a lie.” She turned and picked up a small, worn satchel from behind the barrel. “Here. Food. A little money. A map. The carrier’s name is Dobson. Tell him Elspeth sent you. He will ask no questions.”

I took the satchel. The weight of it, of the packet, of the decision, settled on my shoulders.

“Why are you doing this?” I asked again.

She shuttered the lantern, plunging us into near-darkness. Her voice came out of the black. “Because someone once showed me a kindness when I had no right to expect one. And because I am tired of polishing the silver in the house of a murderer.”

I heard her move away, her footsteps swallowed by the vast dark.

“Go out the way you came. Wait until the watch passes. Then go home. Prepare. Leave nothing behind that you need.”

I stood alone in the consuming dark, the oilskin packet a brand against my ribs. The constraint of Thorne’s silence was gone. In its place was a vast, terrifying freedom. It was the freedom of the cliff’s edge. The freedom of the cut string, vibrating into nothing.

I had come to the warehouse to meet an ending. I had found, instead, a beginning.

The walk back was a journey through a different city. The fog was no longer a muffler, but a cloak. The shadows were no longer threats, but allies. My mind, so long frozen, was a torrent of plans, of fears, of grim calculations. I would write a letter for my father. I would take my lute—not for music, but for disguise, the case a plausible reason for a journey. I would wear my oldest, darkest clothes. I would become a ghost in truth.

I slipped into the house of the Red Door like a thief. The workshop was a pool of deeper shadow. I did not look at the decoy lute. I went to my own instrument, cased in its corner. I opened the case. The lute lay nestled in velvet, a creature of potential song. I lifted it out. From the compartment in the lid, I took the few things I needed: a set of spare strings, a tuning key, a cloth. I placed Elspeth’s oilskin packet inside, against the wood of the lid, and replaced the velvet lining. It fit perfectly. A secret in the belly of a song.

Then I sat at my small desk and lit a single candle. I took out a sheet of paper.

*Father,* I wrote.

*I have gone to seek a new teacher for my music. Do not look for me. Do not fear for me. The silence here was killing the song in me. I have chosen a different kind of quiet, one I must walk into alone.*

*Keep the workshop. Keep making beautiful things. It is the purest truth we have.*

*When I have found what I am listening for, I will return.*

*Your Isabeau.*

It was a lie wrapped in a truth. He would understand the deeper meaning. He would know I had not gone for music. He would know I had gone to fight the silence that had taken his son. He would be terrified. He would be proud.

I left the letter on my pillow. I changed into a stout wool dress and my most serviceable boots. I placed the few coins I had of my own into a purse with Elspeth’s money. As the deepest part of the night settled over Kirkwall, I sat on my bed, my hand on the lute case, and waited for the first, faint grey hint of dawn to bleed through the fog at my window.

This was the choice. Not between playing and breaking. But between being an instrument and being a hand. Between resonance and action. Thorne owned all the expected notes. So I would play the one he could not anticipate. The note from outside his scale. The note of a sister with proof in her hands and nothing left to lose.

When the time came, I stood. I took the lute case. I did not look back at my room. I crept down the stairs, past my father’s door. I paused there, listening to the sound of his sleep-breath. I laid my forehead against the cool wood for a single, stolen second.

Then I went down into the workshop, to the back door that led to the lane. I unlatched it. The cold, fog-laden air rushed in, smelling of the sea, of distance, of a terrible and necessary freedom.

I stepped out. I did not close the door softly. I let the latch fall with a solid, final *click*.

It was the sound of a string, pulled taut to its breaking point. It was the sound of a key, turning in a lock from the inside.

And as I vanished into the grey, swallowing dawn, I finally knew what sound the cut string would make.

It would make the sound of a first footstep on a long, hard road. It would make the sound of a story, refusing to end.



---

# Chapter 20

## **CHAPTER 20**

The road was a scar of churned mud and sharp, flinty stone, cutting through a landscape that had forgotten the concept of green. It was not a road built for destinations, but for endurance. It ran east, away from the sea’s damp breath, into a silence so profound it felt like a physical weight in the ears. This was the hinterland, the place the maps called the Barren Marches. The place where Lucas had last been seen.

I walked. The lute case, slung across my back, was no longer an instrument. It was a coffin for my old life, or a shell I carried on my shoulders. Its familiar weight was the only anchor in a world that had dissolved into this monochrome expanse of grey rock and bleached, rattling grass. Elspeth’s coins were a cold secret against my skin. The proof—the damning ledger page folded small and tucked into the lining of my bodice—was a coal I could feel burning through the wool and linen, branding me with its truth.

I had been walking for two days. The first night, I had slept in the lee of a tumbledown shepherd’s bothy, the roof long gone, the walls offering scant protection from a wind that spoke in a thin, relentless whine. I had not slept so much as passed out, my body a collection of new aches. The second day, the road began to climb, and the fog gave way to a hard, clear cold that made the distant peaks look like teeth against a porcelain sky.

I was not alone on the road. There were others: a carter with a broken axle, cursing at his patient mule; a family walking with the hollow-eyed gait of those leaving one ruin for another; a pair of King’s messengers on lathered horses who passed me in a spray of grit, their eyes sliding over me as if I were a stone. I was a woman alone, and that made me invisible, or a target. I kept my head down, my stride purposeful, my hand never far from the small, sharp boning knife I had taken from the workshop. It was a tool for purfling, for delicate work. I had reshaped its purpose in my mind. It was now a thing for cutting.

On the afternoon of the second day, I saw the crossroads.

It was marked by a single, finger-like standing stone, so weathered its carvings were just phantom suggestions under the moss. Three paths met there. The one I was on, continuing east into the rising hills. A narrower track coiling north, into a dense pine forest that smelled of resin and shadow. And a third, little more than a sheep trail, striking south across a blasted heath.

According to the tinker I’d bought a heel of bread from that morning, the hamlet of Greywater—the last place Lucas’s trail had been faintly warm—lay to the north, a half-day’s walk into the pines. But as I stood at the junction, the stone a cold sentinel at my back, a different pull tugged at my bones. It came from the south.

It was not a sound. It was an absence. A vacuum where the wind itself seemed to die. The heath there was flat and studded with gorse, rolling away to a line of low, rounded hills. Nothing moved. No bird crossed that slice of sky. It was a silence I recognised. It was the silence of my father’s workshop after the news of Lucas. It was the silence Thorne’s money had bought. It was a cultivated quiet, a thing that had been *made*.

The ledger page burned against my breast. *Eastgate Holdings. Excavation & Reclamation. Site Theta.* No location given. Just a column of numbers, payments for “quieting services” and “site security,” amounts that could buy a man’s life or a magistrate’s blindness. Site Theta. It felt like a word that belonged to this desolation.

My mind, trained to find the hidden harmony in disparate notes, heard the dissonance. The tinker’s directions were the expected note. The logical path. Go to Greywater, ask after the handsome, troubled lute-player with the van der Hagen smile. Follow the crumbs of gossip. It was what Thorne would expect a searching sister to do. He would have people there. People paid to watch, to misdirect, to offer a false trail that led to a cliff’s edge.

The silence to the south was the note outside his scale.

I turned my back on the north road and its promise of witness. I stepped off the main track and onto the sheep path.

The quality of the world changed within a hundred paces. The crunch of gravel underfoot became the damp, yielding press of peat. The wind’s whine vanished, swallowed by that immense, watchful stillness. The only sounds were my own: the rustle of my skirts, the creak of the case straps, the rhythm of my breath. I was a trespasser in a place that demanded silence. I tried to give it. I walked as I had once crept past my father’s door at night, each footfall a conscious act of theft.

The heath gave way to the first of the low hills. As I climbed its flank, the reason for the silence became clear. The land was sick. The grass was a brittle, whitish yellow, clinging to a thin skin of soil over cracked, blue-grey stone. Patches of ground were stained a rusty orange, as if the earth itself were bleeding a slow, metallic poison. A trickle of water seeped from a fissure, not clear, but milky and opaque, leaving a chalky deposit on the rocks. I had heard miners talk of such places. Bad ground. Cursed rock.

At the crest of the hill, I dropped to my hands and knees, then lay flat, inching forward to peer over the rim.

The valley below was not natural. It was a wound.

It was a vast, terraced gouge in the earth, a inverted ziggurat of raw cliffs and precarious wooden scaffolding. At its base, maybe two hundred feet down, dark pools of stagnant water glittered dully. Tracks for mine carts, like black scars, ran along the terraces, converging on a cluster of rough timber buildings that clung to the far side of the pit. A chimney belched a thin, greasy smoke that did not rise but clung to the valley floor in a pall. Figures moved, ant-like, along the terraces. The distant *clank* of metal on stone, muffled by the depth, reached me as a dull, arrhythmic heartbeat.

This was no simple mine. It was an excavation. A *reclamation*. Site Theta.

And there, near the largest building, was a sight that stopped the air in my lungs: a tall, wooden framework, like a gallows or a giant’s drying rack. From it hung a series of iron cages. Birdcages for men. Most were empty, swinging slightly in the faint, toxic breeze. In one, a dark shape slumped against the bars.

My fingers dug into the cold peat. The proof in my bodice was no longer an abstract. It was this. It was the ledger’s “quieting services” swinging in that cage. It was the “site security” patrolling the terraces with cudgels and crossbows.

I do not know how long I lay there, the cold seeping through my wool dress, my eyes mapping the patterns of movement. The guards changed at predictable intervals. A cart, piled with dark rock, was hauled from a tunnel mouth by a line of bent figures. The cage was ignored. The figure in it did not move.

Logic said it could not be Lucas. Thorne was not a man to keep evidence alive in so conspicuous a place. This was a lesson, a piece of theatre for the other ants in the pit. A warning of the cost of complaint, of failure, of resistance.

But my heart, that stubborn, untuned instrument, hammered a different rhythm. *He is here. You are close. The silence is loudest where he is.*

I could not go down by daylight. I was a single, stark note against the bleached hillside. I would be seen, questioned, and added to the ledger’s next column of expenses. I had to wait for the cover of night, for the shift in the light that turned details into shadows.

I wriggled backward from the crest and found a depression where the hill slumped into a rocky outcrop. It was sheltered from the worst of the wind and hidden from the valley’s view. I sat with my back against stone, the lute case across my knees. I ate the last of my bread. It tasted of dust and the metallic tang of the air. I drank a little water from my skin. I waited.

The day bled away slowly, the sky fading from porcelain to pewter to a deep, bruised purple. Lamps were lit in the timber buildings below, weak yellow eyes in the gathering dark. The ant-like movements ceased. The *clank-clank* of industry died, replaced by the deeper silence, now punctuated by distant, indistinct shouts and, once, a sharp cry that was cut off abruptly.

When full dark settled, it was not true dark. A sliver of moon cast a sickly gleam on the milky pools below. The smoke from the chimney glowed a faint, infernal orange against the black. It was a hellscape, lit by its own internal fires.

I stood, my limbs stiff and protesting. I left the lute case in the depression, hidden behind the largest rock. It was too cumbersome, too identifiable. I kept only my knife, my water skin, and the proof. I was no longer a luthier carrying her craft. I was a thief in the night, come to steal back what was mine.

I picked my way down the slope, using the scree and scrub for cover, moving with a slow, deliberate care I had learned from a lifetime of handling fragile, precious things. The air grew thicker, warmer as I descended, carrying the smells of damp rot, crushed rock, and something acrid—chemical, like vinegar and spoiled eggs.

I reached the first terrace. The ground was littered with rubble and discarded tools: a broken shovel, a cracked bucket. I stayed close to the cliff face, a shadow among deeper shadows. My goal was the cluster of buildings, and the cage beside them.

The mine works were a labyrinth of shadows and sudden, yawning holes. I passed the black mouth of a tunnel, feeling a chill, damp breath exhale from its depths. I ducked under the tracks of a cart, the iron rails cold to the touch. The sounds of the camp were clearer now: the snort of a horse from a paddock, the murmur of voices from a bunkhouse, a burst of rough laughter.

I was close. I could see the shape of the cage now, a blacker cut-out against the lesser black of the sky. The figure within was still.

A guard patrolled between the buildings, a lantern swinging from his hand. I pressed myself into a niche between two stacks of timber, holding my breath. The pool of lantern-light washed over the ground before me, illuminating chips of stone, a twisted nail. The guard’s boots crunched past. He was humming a tavern tune, off-key and listless. He did not look into the shadows.

When he rounded the corner of a shed, I moved. A quick, darting run across open ground, to the base of the tall framework that held the cages. The wood was rough and splintered under my palms. Up close, the structure reeked of rust and human misery.

I looked up. The cage was ten feet above me. I could see the shape more clearly now. A man, curled on his side on the bare iron floor. He was not moving.

There was no ladder. But the framework was criss-crossed with braces and supports. It was a climb. I tucked my skirts into my belt, tested a lower crossbeam. It held. I began to climb.

It was not graceful. It was a feral, desperate scrambling. Splinters drove into my hands. My boots slipped on the greasy wood. My heart was a frantic drum in my throat, so loud I was sure it would summon the guard. I climbed past the empty cages, their doors hanging open like slack jaws. The higher I went, the more the whole structure seemed to sway, groaning softly with my weight.

I reached the level of his cage. I clung to the support beside it, my feet braced on a lower beam. I was level with the bars.

“Lucas?” The word was a breath, a ghost of sound.

The figure did not stir.

I reached through the bars. My fingers brushed wool, cold and damp. I found a shoulder, gave it a gentle shake. “Please.”

A low moan. A shift. The figure rolled slowly onto its back.

The weak moonlight fell across a face that was not my brother’s.

It was a stranger, gaunt and bearded, his skin pale and waxy. His eyes were open, but they stared past me, through me, at some private horror on the ceiling of his iron world. His lips were cracked and bleeding. He smelled of sweat, sickness, and despair.

My hope, that fragile, foolish string I had been tuning since I left Kirkwall, snapped. The dissonance was a physical pain in my chest. I had followed the wrong note. I was in the heart of the silence, and Lucas was not here.

The man’s eyes focused, minutely. They found my face in the dark. A spark of confusion, then a terrible, urgent intelligence flared in them. His hand, shockingly fast, shot out and clamped around my wrist where it rested on the bar. His grip was a feverish vice.

“Not here,” he rasped, his voice the sound of stone grinding on stone. “The quiet room. The deep tunnel. They take the troublemakers… the ones who ask questions… to the quiet room.”

He was trying to tell me something. To warn me.
“Who? Who asks questions?”

“The musician,” he whispered, and his eyes gleamed with a pity that froze my blood. “The one with the clever hands. He asked about the blue stone. The stone that sings. They took him below. Weeks ago.” His grip tightened, a final, desperate transmission. “You cannot get in. Only the foreman has the key. And the… the quiet man.”

“What quiet man?”

But his strength was gone. The spark guttered. His hand fell away from my wrist, leaving a ghost of its heat. His eyes emptied again, returning to their private void. “The one who makes no sound when he walks,” he murmured to the night. “The one who listens to the rock.”

A boot crunched on gravel below.

I froze, plastered against the framework. The guard was back, his lantern bobbing. He stopped directly beneath the cages. He set the lantern down, fumbled with his trousers, and relieved himself against a post, humming the same dull tune.

I could not move. I could not breathe. The reek of urine rose through the still air. He finished, shook himself, picked up his lantern. He did not look up. He walked away, the light receding.

My limbs were trembling with strain and adrenaline. I had my answer. Lucas was not in a cage. He was in a “quiet room” in a “deep tunnel.” He had asked the wrong question about the wrong stone. And there was a quiet man.

I had to get down. I had to think. This was no longer a rescue. It was an infiltration. I needed a key. I needed to understand the geography of hell.

I began the careful, trembling descent. My mind was a whirl of terrible images: Lucas in a dark hole, Lucas listening to a stone that sang, Lucas facing a man who made no sound. The proof against my skin was useless here. Here, the only law was the foreman’s key and the quiet man’s will.

My foot found the last beam. I dropped the final few feet to the ground, landing softly in a crouch.

And found I was not alone.

A man stood five paces away, in the deep shadow between two piles of ore. He was not the guard. He was tall, thin, dressed in dark, close-fitting clothes that seemed to drink the feeble light. He held no lantern. He made no sound. He was simply *there*, a part of the silence I had sensed from the crossroads. He had been waiting.

He took a single step forward. The moonlight caught the side of his face. It was narrow, austere, devoid of expression. His eyes were dark pools. He looked at me not with surprise or anger, but with a cold, clinical interest, like a naturalist observing a new species of insect.

He was the quiet man.

My hand went to the boning knife at my waist. I drew it. The small blade looked pathetic, a toy against the consuming dark.

He did not react to the knife. His head tilted, just slightly. He was listening. Not to me, but to the space around me, to the night, to the very air I disturbed by breathing.

Then he spoke. His voice was soft, dry, and perfectly modulated. It did not seem to come from him, but from the silence itself.

“You are off-key,” he said.

He took another step. I backed away, my shoulder hitting the rough wood of the cage framework. There was nowhere to run. The terrace behind me was a dead end, dropping off to the next level of the pit.

“This is a place of precise extraction,” the quiet man said, his tone conversational, instructive. “A process. You are an impurity. A vibration in the wrong frequency.”

He was between me and the way I had come. Between me and the hillside, and freedom, and the lute case waiting in the rocks. I thought of the ledger. *Quieting services.* This was what they had bought. This man, who turned people into impurities, into resolved dissonances.

I tightened my grip on the knife. It was not a tool for purfling anymore. It was the only note I had left to play.

“Where is my brother?” My voice, by some miracle, did not shake.

The quiet man’s lips curved in a smile that did not touch his eyes. It was a crack in marble. “The musician? He found a note he could not unhear. He is… being retuned.” He took another step, now only three paces away. “You have his look. The same restless hands. The same inability to leave the silence alone.”

He moved then. Not with a rush, but with an appalling, fluid economy. There was no warning tension in his shoulders, no shift of weight. One moment he was still; the next, he was within arm’s reach, his own hand—long-fingered, pale—reaching not for my knife, but for my throat.

I did not think. I played the only note I had.

I dropped low, under his reaching arm, and I did not slash with the knife. I stabbed. As my father had taught me when setting a delicate soundpost, with a firm, direct pressure. I put the narrow, sharp point of the boning knife into the side of the quiet man’s thigh, just above the knee, and I pushed until I felt it grate against something that was not flesh.

He made a sound. Not a cry. A soft, surprised exhale, like a man who has found a flaw in a perfect calculation. His fluid motion broke. He staggered half a step, his hand going to his leg. His dark eyes found mine, and in them, for the first time, I saw not clinical interest, but a flicker of pure, icy outrage.

I did not wait. I twisted the knife free—a terrible, wet sound—and I ran.

Not back the way I had come, but along the terrace, toward the black mouth of the nearest tunnel. It was the only path not blocked by him. It was a descent into the belly of the wound. Into the deep tunnels. Into the quiet rooms.

His footsteps did not come pounding after me. There was only a swift, soft scuffling, and then a low, venomous command hissed into the night. “*Find her.*”

I plunged into the tunnel’s mouth. The damp, chemical breath of the earth swallowed me. The moonlight vanished, replaced by a blackness so total it was a solid thing. I stumbled forward, one hand on the cold, slimy wall, the other still clutching the warm, wet knife.

I had cut the string. I had drawn blood from the silence itself. And now I was inside the instrument, in the dark resonant chamber, with no idea of its shape or its end. The only sound was the frantic hammer of my own heart, and the distant, dripping water, and the growing certainty that I had just turned myself from a searcher into prey.

The road had ended. The maze had begun. And somewhere in this labyrinth of stone and silence, my brother was waiting, listening for a note that would never come.

Unless I brought it.



---

# Chapter 21

## **CHAPTER 21**

The dark was a kind of silence. It had texture, and weight, and a taste like rust and wet limestone. It pressed against my eyes, against my mouth, as I stumbled forward, one hand skating over the slick, irregular wall. The other hand was a fist around the knife handle. The warmth of the quiet man’s blood had cooled to a sticky film between my fingers.

I did not run. Running in such blackness was an invitation to a broken ankle, a cracked skull. I moved as a blind thing moves, by touch and by terrible necessity, each step a negotiation with the unseen. The tunnel floor sloped downward, a steady, insistent decline. The air grew colder, damper, carrying with it the mineral tang of deep groundwater and something else—a faint, sweet-sour note of decay, like forgotten flowers in a cellar.

I listened until my ears ached. Behind me, no footfalls echoed. Only the perpetual, patient drip of water from the ceiling, each drop a tiny percussion in the void. *Find her*, he had said. The command had not been for me. It had been for the dark itself. For the other things that moved in it.

My breath came in short, sharp clouds that the blackness swallowed whole. I forced it to slow, to deepen. Panic was a string wound too tight; it would snap, and the note would give me away. I was inside the instrument now. Every sound I made was a vibration in its body. I had to move as a dissonance moves, careful and unresolved.

I counted my steps. Two hundred and seventeen. The wall under my hand changed. The rough-hewn stone gave way to something smoother, dressed. A corner. I felt my way around it, and the tunnel branched. One path continued the descent, the chill from it palpable. The other felt level, and a faint, almost imperceptible draft brushed my face, carrying a different smell—old smoke, and tallow, and the ghost of human habitation.

The quiet rooms.

I took the level path. The draft was a thread. I would follow it.

The darkness began to thin. Not with light, but with a suggestion of space. The close, suffocating pressure of the tunnel walls receded. I was in a larger chamber. I could feel it in the emptiness that echoed back my shallow breaths. I stopped, crouching low, straining to see. Gradually, shapes coalesced from the gloom—the hulking outlines of stacked crates, the angular form of a trestle table, the pale suggestion of barrels.

A storeroom. Or a staging post.

Then, a light. Not a lamp or a candle, but a dull, greenish luminescence that seeped from a doorway on the far side of the chamber. It was the colour of spoiled meat, of lichen on a sunless rock. It painted the edges of things in a sickly halo.

I crept toward it, keeping to the shadows of the crates. The floor here was dry, dust gritting under my boots. The smell of tallow grew stronger, undercut by that sweet decay. The doorway was not a door, but an archway hung with a heavy curtain of what looked like oiled leather, cut into long, vertical strips. The green light bled through the gaps between them.

From beyond the curtain came a sound. A low, rhythmic scraping. Metal on stone. A pause. Then a soft, wet *plop*.

I edged to the side of the archway, pressing my back against the cold wall. I brought my eye to a gap between two leather strips.

The room beyond was small, a cell carved from the living rock. The green light came from a cluster of fungus growing in a ceramic bowl on a central table—a cold, witchy glow that made a mockery of illumination. It revealed just enough to deepen the terror.

A man sat on a stool at the table. His back was to me. He wore a dark, close-fitting jerkin. His head was bent in concentration. On the table before him lay an array of tools on a stained cloth: probes, slender saws, clamps with fine, cruel teeth. Beside them rested a lump of dark, wet clay on a slate tile.

The man picked up a metal spatula. He resumed his scraping, methodical, precise, collecting the clay shavings onto the blade. He transferred them to a small bronze mortar. He selected a pestle and began to grind. The *plop* had been water, added from a jug. The sound that followed was the muffled, grinding paste of earth and liquid.

He was not making a poultice. He was mixing pigment.

I knew this. I had ground malachite for verdigris, lapis lazuli for ultramarine. This was the same patient, physical alchemy. But this clay had a familiar, faint sheen in the ghastly light. A reddish-black gleam. My stomach turned to ice.

It was the clay from the wound in the hillside. The clay that had swallowed Lucas’s violin case.

The man set the pestle down. He reached for something else on the table, just out of my line of sight. His shoulders shifted as he worked. There was a series of delicate, clicking sounds. The tap of a fine brush against the rim of a cup. A long, slow, exhale of concentration.

Then he leaned back, just slightly, to assess his work.

I saw what was on the table before him.

It was a mask. Not a full mask, but a half-piece, meant to cover from the brow to the upper lip. It was cast of a pale, creamy ceramic—porcelain, or fine bisque. It was blank, unfinished. And onto its smooth, empty surface, the man was painting features.

With the clay-pigment, thinned to a ink, he was painting a pair of closed eyes. The brushwork was exquisite, delicate. The lashes were fine as hairs, the lids rendered with a tender, sleeping weight. They were Lucas’s eyes. The exact set of them, the specific arch of the brow I had seen a thousand times across the breakfast table, in concentration over a music sheet, in laughter.

This was no crude likeness. It was a portrait in mineral and earth. A remembrance.

My blood became a silent scream in my veins. I clutched the knife so hard the handle groaned in my grip.

The man dipped his brush again. He began to paint the lips. A soft, neutral line. Not smiling, not frowning. The lips of someone at peace. Of someone who would never speak again.

A memory surfaced, sharp as a splinter. My father’s voice, years ago, explaining the art of the luthier. *The wood must be seasoned, Isabeau. It must have lived, and then it must have died, and then it must wait in the silence. Only then can it hold a note without warping. Only then can it sing.*

The clay was the seasoning. The silence was the waiting.

They were not just hiding Lucas. They were preparing him. For what?

The man set his brush down. He reached for a small, sharp awl. With infinite care, he began to prick tiny holes along the edge of the mask, where it would be tied or fastened. The *tick-tick-tick* of the awl tip on ceramic was the loudest sound in the world.

I could not stay here. I could not watch this sacrament of erasure. But neither could I turn away. This was the heart of the maze. This was where the string had been cut.

I needed to see his face. The face of the man painting my brother’s silence.

I shifted my weight, seeking a better angle. My boot scuffed a pebble.

The sound was a gunshot in the quiet.

The man froze. The awl hovered. He did not turn. He did not startle. He simply ceased all motion, becoming a part of the room’s furniture.

Then, slowly, he laid the awl down on the cloth. He placed his palms flat on the table. He spoke, and his voice was a dry, papery rustle, utterly devoid of inflection.

“You are in the tuning room,” he said. “The air is unsettled.”

He knew I was there. He had known, perhaps, from the moment I had disturbed the draft at the curtain.

I did not move. I did not breathe.

“You have brought a discord,” the papery voice continued. “A vibration. It must be resolved, or the harmony cannot be achieved.”

He stood up from the stool. He was tall, gaunt. He did not turn to face me. Instead, he walked to the far wall of the cell, where the green light did not reach. He vanished into the deeper shadow.

I heard a latch click. A door I had not seen swung inward, revealing not another room, but a narrow, ascending staircase cut into the rock. A colder, cleaner draft swept in, carrying the distant, salt-kissed smell of the outside world. Of the Firth.

He paused at the threshold, a silhouette against a slightly less profound blackness.

“The instrument is almost ready,” he said, his voice already fading, as if he were speaking from a great distance. “The final note requires a choice. You can bring it. Or you can become it.”

Then he was gone, the door clicking shut behind him.

I stood, paralysed, in the lee of the leather curtain. The painted eyes of the mask stared blankly across the cell. The choice. The final note.

They wanted something from me. Not just my silence, but my participation. My brother’s face, painted in the clay of his own prison, was the bait. The hook.

I pushed through the leather strips into the cell. The green fungus-light made my skin look corpse-like. I approached the table. Up close, the mask was even more horrifying in its beauty. The brushwork was masterful. It was Lucas, and it was a shell. A vessel waiting to be filled.

Beside the mortar of clay-pigment lay the awl. And beside the awl, a small, folded square of parchment, weighted down by a smooth river stone.

I picked it up. Unfolded it.

A map. Drawn in the same, meticulous hand that had painted the eyes. It charted a section of the tunnels. A single path was inked in a darker line, leading from this chamber upward, through a series of chambers with names written in a tiny, precise script: *The Choir. The Resonance Chamber. The Bridge.*

At the top of the map was a final chamber. It had no name. Only a small, drawn symbol: a single, eighth note, rendered with a perfectly oval notehead and a straight, unwavering stem.

Below the map, two lines of text:

*The instrument awaits its player.*
*The choice is the tune.*

It was an invitation. A score placed on a stand.

He had left it for me. The gaunt man with the papery voice. He had heard my discord, and instead of silencing it, he was directing it. Channeling it toward a specific, final chord.

I looked from the map to the mask. The choice. I could follow the map into the heart of their machine, whatever it was. Or I could flee back into the blind dark, and leave Lucas to become the empty, beautiful thing on the table.

There was no choice. Not really.

I took the map. I left the mask staring its blind, porcelain stare. I went to the door in the far wall. The latch lifted without a sound. The staircase beyond was hewn from rough rock, a spiral twisting upward. The salt-air draft was stronger here, a promise of sky.

I began to climb. The knife was a dead weight in my hand. The map was a live coal in my pocket. Each step was a note in a scale I did not know, leading to a cadence I could not foresee.

The staircase ended in another door, this one of aged, heavy oak, banded with iron. It was unlocked. I pushed it open, inch by inch, and emerged not into the open air, but into another kind of space.

It was a long, narrow gallery, like the choir loft of a church carved underground. A series of arched openings lined one side, overlooking a vast, black emptiness below. From the abyss came a sound—a deep, almost subsonic hum, the vibration of the earth itself, or of some great engine buried within it. The air here was dry, charged, tasting of ozone and hot metal.

This was the Choir.

I moved to the nearest arch and looked down.

The chamber below was colossal, a natural cavern put to a monstrous purpose. In its centre, suspended by a cat’s cradle of thick ropes and iron chains from the distant ceiling, hung a structure. It was not a building. It was a framework, a skeleton of dark timber and gleaming brass, shaped like the immense, inverted belly of a lute or a viol. A soundbox. Dozens of strings, thick as cables, glinted in the light of scattered, caged phosphorescent fungi, running from the framework down into the blackness of the pit below, where they must be anchored to some unimaginable tension.

Around the edges of the chamber, figures moved. Small, dark, and silent as ants. They tended to smaller devices—banks of metal plates, rows of tuned bells, arrays of glass cylinders filled with varying levels of water. A laboratory of sound. A factory for silence.

And on a platform that extended partway out over the abyss, directly beside the great hanging soundbox, stood a familiar, solitary figure.

The quiet man. He stood with his hands clasped behind his back, looking up at the monstrous instrument. His leg showed no sign of my knife. He was as still and poised as a tuning fork.

He was waiting. For the player. For the final note.

My path, according to the map, led along this gallery, through a door at the far end marked *The Resonance Chamber*. That was the way forward. That was the way to the final, unnamed room.

But to get there, I would have to traverse the entire length of the gallery, in full view of anyone who might look up from the chamber floor. I was a shadow among shadows here, but movement would betray me.

I had to cross. There was no other path.

I took a breath, willed my body to be fluid, to be part of the stone. I stepped out from the shelter of the doorway and began to move, keeping low, close to the wall.

I had taken five steps when the music began.

It was not music as I knew it. It had no melody, no harmony. It was a single, pure, profound tone that seemed to come from everywhere and nowhere at once. It was the hum of the cavern deepened, purified, given intent. It vibrated in the rock under my feet, in the fillings of my teeth, in the marrow of my bones. It was the note of the earth’s own chord.

The effect on the chamber below was immediate. The scurrying figures stopped. They turned as one, like sunflowers to a vile sun, toward the source of the sound. The quiet man on the platform did not turn. He merely inclined his head, a listener in perfect accord.

The tone held. It was not loud, but it was immense. It was a pressure. I felt it pushing against my eardrums, against my thoughts. The frantic, sharp edges of my fear began to blur. My purpose—Lucas, the knife, the map—seemed to recede, to become small and distant, like a problem from a childhood dream. A profound, empty calm threatened to wash over me. This was the silence they manufactured. Not an absence, but a presence so total it obliterated everything else.

I clutched the knife handle, pressed the point into the pad of my thumb. The sharp, specific pain was a pinprick in the vast blanket of the tone. It anchored me. I forced my legs to move. Another step. Another.

I was halfway across the gallery. The tone shifted. A second note joined it, a perfect fifth above. The interval was clean, vast, resonating through the great hanging soundbox until the very air seemed to thicken with congruence. The two notes were a wall. A perfect, immovable structure of sound.

Beneath it, I saw one of the dark figures below slowly raise its hands to its ears, then lower them. A gesture of surrender. Of bliss.

I could not let it take me. I thought of Lucas’s eyes, painted in clay. I thought of the knife grating on bone. I thought of the letter smelling of salt and bad news. These were my dissonances. My flaws in their perfect calculation. I held them before me like a shield of broken glass.

I reached the far door. It was marked with a brass plaque, etched with the same symbol from the map: an eighth note. The Resonance Chamber.

I grasped the iron handle. It was cold. As the two great notes swelled, filling the universe, I pulled the door open and slipped through.

The door shut behind me, muting the monumental music to a deep, pervasive throb. I was in a small, circular room. The walls, floor, and ceiling were clad in sheets of hammered copper, now verdigrised to a mottled green and black. In the centre of the room stood a single, stone pedestal. On it rested a violin.

It was Lucas’s violin.

Not his case, but the instrument itself. The one I had last seen in his hands the night he played for my father. The one that had been sealed in the clay. Its wood glowed with a soft, familiar lustre in the light of a single, shuttered lantern hanging from the ceiling. It was whole. Unharmed. Strung, and a bow lay beside it on the pedestal, its horsehair clean and taut.

It was a miracle. It was a trap.

I approached it, my boots whispering on the copper floor. The air here was still, dead. The thunderous resonance from the Choir was a distant storm here, felt more than heard.

This was the choice. The gaunt man’s words echoed. *You can bring it. Or you can become it.*

They had brought me here, herded me with threats and maps and painted masks, to this room, to this instrument. They wanted me to play. They wanted the final note from Lucas’s violin, played by my hand. It would complete something. It would activate some part of their terrible machine.

I reached out. My fingers, still stained with the quiet man’s blood, hovered over the smooth curve of the spruce belly. I did not touch it.

To play would be to collaborate. To add my voice to their chorus of silence. To turn Lucas’s music, our last, frayed connection, into a tool for his own erasure.

But not to play… that was to abandon him to the mask, to the clay, to the becoming. It was to let their perfect, soundless harmony close over him forever.

The throb of the foundational notes pulsed through the copper walls. The violin sat on its pedestal, a silent question. A loaded gun.

I looked at the bow. I looked at my bloody hands.

Somewhere in this labyrinth of stone and silence, my brother was waiting, listening for a note that would never come.

Unless I brought it.

And I knew, with a certainty that was colder than the tunnel’s dark, that the note they wanted me to play would not be a call. It would be a farewell. It would be the sound of a string, cut for the last time.

The door behind me had no handle on the inside.

I was in the resonance chamber. And the instrument was waiting.



---

# Chapter 22

## **CHAPTER 22**

The violin was a still life of temptation. It held the shape of every ghost that had ever haunted our workshop, the curve of my father’s shoulder as he cradled a soundboard, the shadow of Lucas’s chin against a chinrest. It was a memory made wood. To touch it would be to touch him, one last time, through the medium of his craft. They had calculated this. They had built the trap from the very substance of my longing.

My hands remained at my sides. The blood on them was a dry, brown map of a violence I had chosen. It was a better stain than the one they offered.

I turned from the pedestal. The door was a seamless sheet of copper, flush with the wall. No handle, no keyhole, just a faint hairline seam where it met the jamb. A pressure plate, perhaps. Or a mechanism that responded only to sound. I ran my fingers along the seam, feeling for a catch, a variance in temperature, a vibration. There was nothing. The metal was cool and unyielding, singing with the deep, sub-audible hum that permeated the chamber. It was not a door meant to be opened from within. It was a lid.

The room was a perfect cube, perhaps ten paces across. The walls, the floor, the ceiling—all burnished copper, now dulled with a patina of age except where the lantern’s light pooled. In that light, I saw my own reflection repeated into a fractured infinity: a pale, smudged face, dark hair escaping its plait, eyes too large in their sockets. A girl in a cage of her own echoing image.

I walked the perimeter. My boots were soft on the metal, but every step produced a faint, tonal *ping*, absorbed almost instantly by the dense air. This was a room designed to contain resonance, to shape it, to direct it inward. A crucible for sound.

In the corner opposite the door, I found it. A shallow copper basin, set into the floor. It was filled with a dark, viscous liquid that smelled of ozone and wet stone. The Blackwater. A thin tendril of vapour curled from its surface, unhurried. Beside it, laid on the floor, was a clay mask. It was blank, featureless, awaiting an impression. The final, silent offer.

They had thought of everything. The instrument. The door. The water. The mask. A sequence of choices, each one narrowing the path until only their desired outcome remained. Play, or be still. Become music, or become clay.

I knelt by the basin. My reflection in the Blackwater was distorted, a smeared portrait of a stranger. The thrumming of the foundation notes was stronger here, as if the liquid was a conduit. I could feel it in my teeth, in the marrow of my fingers. It was a sound that wanted to replace my heartbeat.

I did not look at the mask.

I stood and went back to the violin. I did not reach for it. I studied it, as my father had taught me to study any instrument before repair. Look with your hands in your pockets, he’d say. See what it is before you ask it to be something else.

It was Lucas’s work. The F-hole scrolls were cut with his particular, impatient flair—a fraction too deep on the lower curve. The purfling was inlaid with a hair’s breadth of discrepancy near the bottom bout, a mistake he’d laughed about, called a signature. It was his. And yet, it was not. It had been buried in the silent earth of this place, steeped in the Blackwater and the pulse of their machine. It had been played by the gaunt man in a performance that had un-made a room. It was no longer just a violin. It was a vessel. A key.

The bow lay beside it, the wood a deep, rich pernambuco. I saw a single, white hair, loose from the hank. It could have been mine, from when Lucas would snatch a strand to test the tension, laughing as I swatted at him.

A cold clarity settled over me, colder than the chamber’s air. They wanted a note. A specific frequency, born of this specific wood, drawn by a specific hand—a hand that shared his blood, his craft, his memory. They needed my compliance to make it true. My will, bending to theirs, to complete the circuit. To seal the silence.

But sound was not so obedient. Sound was wild. It bounced. It fractured. It found cracks.

I looked at the copper walls. I looked at the violin. I thought of the Choir, arrayed in their terrible harmony above me. I thought of the quiet man, his blood on my hands, his last act a disruption. A wrong note in their perfect scale.

I would not play their note.

But I would make a sound.

I took off my leather apron, stiff with grime and blood. I folded it into a thick, padded square. I walked to the centre of the room, directly under the hanging lantern. I placed the folded apron on the copper floor.

Then I went to the pedestal. I did not hesitate. I picked up the violin.

The wood was warm. It vibrated with a latent energy, a captured echo waiting to be released. It felt like holding a sleeping bird that dreamed of thunder. I tucked it under my chin, the familiar curve a shock of intimacy that made my throat tighten. The scent of its varnish—my father’s recipe, a mix of mastic and amber—rose faintly through the odours of ozone and metal. A ghost of home.

I picked up the bow. I tightened the frog, the mechanism moving with a well-oiled silence. I tested the hair with my thumb. It bit.

I did not stand formally. I stood loosely, my feet planted on the padded leather. I closed my eyes. I did not think of a melody. I did not think of Lucas’s unfinished song. I thought of a single point of pressure. The meeting of hair and string. The birth of a friction.

I drew the bow.

The note was not pure. It was not the clear, singing tone of a well-tuned instrument in a dry room. It was a complex, immediate snarl. The copper walls seized it the moment it left the string. They multiplied it, reflected it, fed it back upon itself. The single note became a swarm, a building, metallic shriek that seemed to come from every surface at once. The air in the room tightened. The lantern above me swayed, its light jittering.

I did not stop. I leaned into it. I changed the pressure, digging the bow into the string, producing a gritty, distorted harmonic that clawed at the ears. The sound was physical now. It vibrated up through the soles of my boots, into my bones. The copper basin rippled, the Blackwater shivering. The featureless mask seemed to tremble.

This was not music. This was noise. A deliberate, violent cacophony.

I sawed at the strings, a ragged, rhythmless assault. The violin, built for beauty, protested with wails and groans. The feedback from the room intensified, a rising wall of sonic pressure. My teeth rattled. The single note they wanted was lost, drowned in a sea of dissonance.

I played the violin as if it were a tool for demolition.

The door did not open. But something else happened.

The deep, foundational hum from below faltered. For a heartbeat, the pervasive thrumming ceased, replaced by the dying echo of my screeching strings. Then it returned, but changed—a beat out of sync, a jarring discordance with itself. I had introduced a flaw. A splinter in their monolithic silence.

From beyond the copper walls, a new sound filtered down. Not the organised resonance of the Choir, but a confusion. A shout, muffled. A scrape of stone. A single, clear, ringing note from a bell that sounded nothing like their harmonic metal.

They had heard me. Not the note they wanted, but the rebellion I had made instead.

The door hissed.

It was not a swing, but a withdrawal. The seamless copper panel slid sideways into the wall with a soft, oiled sigh, revealing the dark tunnel beyond.

In the doorway stood the gaunt man. He was not wearing his mask. His face was pale, drawn, his eyes burning with a cold, furious light. Behind him, the tunnel was in disarray. One of the iron sconces lay on its side on the floor, its green chemical fire guttering. A figure in grey robes was slumped against the wall, hands pressed to the sides of their head.

The gaunt man did not speak. He looked from me, to the violin in my hands, to the bow held like a weapon. He looked at the copper basin, undisturbed. He looked at the untouched mask.

“You were to play a note,” he said. His voice was hoarse, stripped of its earlier melodic certainty. “One note. To complete the resonance. To make him part of the permanent architecture.”

“I played many notes,” I said. My own voice sounded raw in my throat. “They were all for him. None of them were for you.”

He took a step into the room. The air seemed to grow thinner. “You have destabilised a sequence that has been in motion for decades. You have introduced chaos into a perfect equation.”

“Good.”

He was upon me faster than I could react. One long, skeletal hand shot out and wrenched the violin from my grasp. The other seized my wrist, his fingers like iron bands. He did not take the bow.

“The instrument is tuned. The chamber is primed. The source is here.” He dragged me toward the pedestal. His strength was immense, inhuman. “You will play the note. You will correct the error.”

I fought, digging my heels into the copper, but I was a sapling against a gale. He forced my hand, the one holding the bow, toward the violin he now held out.

“The note is the frequency of his absence,” the gaunt man hissed into my ear. “It is the sound of a memory, perfectly preserved. You will play it, and he will become that. Eternal. Static. Free from the corruption of time and choice.”

My fingers were white on the bow. The horsehair hovered over the string. I could feel the pull of it, the terrible temptation to just do it, to end the struggle, to let the note ring out and perhaps, in some way, keep Lucas safe in amber.

But amber was a tomb.

I went limp. My entire body sagged, a dead weight in his grasp. He staggered, unprepared for the sudden surrender. In that fraction of a second of shifted balance, I twisted. I did not pull away. I drove forward, into him.

I brought the bow up, not to the string, but to his face.

The tip of the bow, the sharp edge of the ivory faceplate, caught him across the eye. He roared, a sound of pure, undiluted fury, and his grip loosened. I tore myself free, stumbling back toward the open door.

He clutched his face. A thin, dark line of blood welled above his brow. He looked at me, his one good eye wide with something beyond anger—shock. The violence was not in the script. The instrument was not to be used as a blade.

“You break everything you touch,” he whispered.

“I am my brother’s sister,” I said.

I turned and ran.

The tunnel was a nightmare of echoes and confusion. The foundational hum was now a sickening, warbling pulse. The green flames in the sconces danced erratically. From somewhere far above, I heard a crash, like stone falling. The Choir’s harmony was broken. My cacophony in the copper chamber had been a stone thrown into their still, black pool.

I did not know where to go. I only knew I had to go up. Up, toward the sound of collapse. Up, toward where Lucas might be.

I passed intersections I did not recognise. I passed open doorways revealing empty cells, workrooms with strange, crystalline apparatuses lying dormant. I passed a grey-robed figure kneeling on the floor, rocking back and forth, hands over their ears.

Then, a familiar turn. The corridor with the cells, where I had been held. Where the quiet man had died. The door to my old cell was open. I glanced in as I passed.

It was not empty.

Lucas stood in the centre of the small room.

He was facing the far wall, his back to me. He was dressed in simple, dark clothes that were not his own. His hair was shorter, roughly cut. He was utterly still.

My breath caught. I stopped in the doorway, my hand on the jamb. “Lucas?”

He did not turn. He did not move.

“Lucas, it’s me.”

Slowly, as if the motion required great effort, he began to turn. His movements were stiff, deliberate. Not like his old, fluid self. It was the turn of someone listening to a distant, difficult instruction.

He faced me.

It was his face. The same sharp bones, the same mouth. But his eyes… they were not his eyes. They were the colour of the Blackwater, dark and depthless, with no light of recognition in them. They looked at me as one might look at a piece of furniture. A familiar, but irrelevant, object.

“Isabeau,” he said. His voice was his, and yet not. It was flat. Toneless. Stripped of all the music that had always lived in it.

He took a step toward me. “You are not in the resonance chamber.”

“I was. I left.”

“You were to play the note.”

“I didn’t play your note.”

A faint ripple passed over his features, a shadow of confusion. “My note is the only note. It is the summation. The full stop.” He tilted his head, a bird-like gesture that was utterly alien to him. “You have introduced variables. The equation is unstable.”

He was speaking, but it wasn’t him. It was the doctrine of this place, spoken through his lips. The clay was already taking the impression.

“Lucas, listen to me. Fight it. Whatever they’ve done, whatever they’re pouring into you… it’s not you. Your music isn’t a full stop. It’s a question. It always was.”

He took another step. We were an arm’s length apart. I could smell the ozone on him, the scent of the Blackwater. “Music is disorder. It is entropy. We are creating order. A perfect, sustained chord against the chaos of time. I am to be a note in that chord. You were to be the finger that placed me.”

“I won’t do it.”

“Then you will be removed from the equation.” He said it with no malice, no regret. A simple statement of fact. “The variable must be cancelled.”

His hand came up. It was not a fist. It was an open palm, aimed at my chest. There was no threat in the gesture, only a terrible, final purpose.

From down the corridor, a sound erupted. Not a note, but a word, shouted with raw, tearing force.

“*ISABEAU!*”

It was my father’s voice.

Lucas froze. The black-water eyes flickered. For an instant, a crack appeared in the perfect, placid surface. A spark of something else—pain, recognition, terror.

He turned his head toward the sound.

In that instant, I did not run to him. I ran past him. I ducked under his still-raised arm and fled the cell, back into the corridor.

My father stood twenty paces away, at the corridor’s end. He held a long, iron tuning lever from the workshop like a quarterstaff. Behind him were faces I knew—Marta, the baker’s wife, her apron stained with soot. Old Jan the cooper, a hammer in his hand. Kees, the printer’s apprentice, clutching a metal type case like a shield. A dozen others from the wynds around the Red Door. Their faces were grim, smudged, alive.

They had come. They had found a way in.

My father’s eyes found mine, a flash of unbearable relief. Then they moved past me, to the figure emerging from the cell behind me.

Lucas stood in the doorway. He looked at the crowd, at the father he had not seen in a year of torment. His blank expression wavered. A tremor went through him.

“Pater?” The word was a child’s whisper, lost and small.

My father’s face crumpled. He took a step forward. “My boy.”

The conflict on Lucas’s face was a silent war. The dark, imposed order against the fragile, human memory. He raised his hands, not in threat, but as if pushing against an invisible weight. “You… should not be here. This is not your harmony.”

“We’re not here for harmony, son,” my father said, his voice thick. “We’re here to bring you home.”

From the depths of the complex, a new sound rose. Not the broken hum, not the echoes of chaos. It was the sound of many feet, moving in unison. A disciplined, rhythmic tread. The grey robes, mobilising. The gaunt man’s correction.

Lucas heard it too. The war in his eyes ended. The blankness slammed down again, harder, final. “There is no home,” he said, the tonelessness returned. “There is only the Chord.”

He turned his black-water eyes on me. “You are the variable. You must be resolved.”

He moved toward me, not with Lucas’s loping stride, but with a dreadful, mechanical certainty.

My father stepped between us, raising the iron lever. “Lucas. Stop.”

Lucas did not stop. He walked into the lever. My father, unwilling to strike his own son, could only hold it as a barrier. Lucas pressed against it, his strength immense, forcing my father back step by step.

The rhythmic tread grew louder. Around the far corner, a line of grey-robed figures appeared. They moved as one, blocking the way out. At their centre was the gaunt man, a strip of dark cloth tied around his injured eye. In his hands, he held Lucas’s violin.

He looked at me, at my father, at our ragged band of rescuers. He looked at Lucas, pressing mindlessly forward.

“The source is compromised,” the gaunt man announced, his voice carrying down the stone corridor. “The variable is active. Initiate dissolution. Silence the discord.”

The grey robes began to advance.

We were trapped between my brother, who was no longer my brother, and the silent, advancing Choir.

My father met my eyes over Lucas’s shoulder. In his face, I saw no fear, only a profound, grieving determination. He gave a slight nod toward the tunnel branching off to our left—a narrow, dark passage I had not noticed.

He would hold the line. He would hold Lucas. He would buy time.

The variable had to run.

I backed away, my heart a hammer against my ribs. I looked once more at Lucas, his face a mask of alien purpose, forcing his own father backward. I looked at my father, planting his feet, the iron lever trembling in his hands.

I turned and fled down the dark, narrow passage.

The last thing I heard was the gaunt man’s voice, cold and clear, giving the final command.

“Take the source. Reset the chamber. We will complete the equation with what remains.”

And the sound of my father, shouting my name not as a warning, but as a farewell.



---

# Chapter 23

## **CHAPTER 23**

The dark passage swallowed me. It was not stone, but earth, held back by ancient, weeping timbers. The air tasted of wet clay and root-rot. The sounds from the corridor behind me—the grunt of my father’s effort, the shuffle of grey robes, the cold command—muffled and died as I ran, bent double, my hands scraping the clammy walls for balance.

I did not think. Thinking was a luxury for those who could afford the weight of a future. I was a variable in flight. A note escaping the measure.

The passage sloped down. Water pooled in the ruts of a long-forgotten cart track beneath my feet. The darkness was not absolute; a faint, sickly luminescence seeped from patches of fungus clinging to the timber braces, painting the tunnel in shades of corpse-green and bruise-purple. It was the light of things that grew in the absence of the sun.

I ran until my lungs burned and my legs shook. I ran until the silence behind me was complete, a verdict. Only then did I stagger to a halt, bracing myself against a timber, my forehead pressed to the cool, fungal glow. I listened. Nothing. No pursuit. They had not followed.

The realisation was a colder terror than the chase.

They had not followed because they did not need to. The source was compromised. The variable was active. I was running, but I was still within the equation. My flight was a parameter they had already accounted for. I was a term to be resolved later, once the constants—my father, the others—had been cancelled out.

I was free to run precisely because it meant nothing.

The thought unmoored me. I slid down the timber to the muddy ground, the damp seeping through my skirts. The hammering of my heart was not a drum of defiance, but the frantic ticking of a mechanism winding down. I had left him. I had left my father to face Lucas, to face the Choir, with nothing but an iron lever and a grieving determination. I had obeyed his final, silent command. I had run.

And for what? To die lost in the dark, a variable erased in solitude?

I fumbled in the pouch at my belt. My fingers closed around two things: the smooth, cold disc of my mother’s wedding ring, and the rough, folded square of parchment. The letter. The one that had started it, smelling of salt and bad news. I could not see it in the gloom, but I did not need to. I knew its words by the scars they had left on my memory.

*If you read this, I am already gone from the city.*

Lucas had run. He had been the variable first. He had fled into the wynds and the taverns, into the arms of the Choir and their terrible music, seeking a freedom from the weight of our name, from the dust of the workshop, from the ghost of our mother. He had sought a constraint that would absolve him of choice. And he had found it. They had given him a new name: the Source. They had played his will like a string, tuning him to their dreadful harmony.

I had judged him for it. I had thought his flight a cowardice, a breaking of faith.

Now I sat in a mud tunnel, fleeing the consequences of my own choices, and understood him perfectly. Freedom was not the absence of walls. It was the terrifying burden of building them yourself, of choosing what to keep in and what to keep out. It was the constant, wearying calculus of it. The Choir offered a release from that calculus. They offered a perfect, silent constraint. A final resolution.

The fungal light pulsed softly. It was a kind of music, this slow, vegetative rhythm. A song of consumption and decay.

I pushed myself to my feet. My legs protested. My mind was a numb, cold thing. But a new thought, small and hard, had formed in the centre of the numbness. Lucas had run *from*. I was running *to*.

He had not finished his letter. The gaunt man had taken him before he could write more. But he had started it. He had tried to speak. He had reached for a connection, however frayed, outside the Choir’s control. That attempt was the flaw in their equation. That was the discord they could not silence.

I was not a variable running to escape the formula. I was the variable running to find the one term they could not solve for: the part of Lucas that had tried to write the letter.

The passage began to narrow, the timbers closer, their groans more personal. The cart track vanished under a slick of black water. I waded through it, the cold biting my ankles. The tunnel curved, and the quality of the sound changed. The drip of water gained an echo. The air moved, carrying a new scent—not rot, but soot, and hot metal, and something acrid I could not name.

I emerged not into another cavern, but onto a narrow ledge. Below me yawned a space so vast the fungal light from my tunnel could not touch its edges. But other lights burned in the deep.

It was a factory of souls.

The chamber was a vertical forge. Iron gantries and stone walkways criss-crossed a central void. From the distant ceiling, chains descended, holding great braziers of greenish fire that cast jumping, malevolent shadows. Upon the walkways, grey-robed figures moved in silent, precise patterns, tending to machinery whose purpose I could barely comprehend. I saw great wheels turning, not driven by water or steam, but by the movement of the robed figures themselves, walking in endless circles within them like hamsters in a clock. I saw bellows the size of houses, breathing with a slow, sickly rhythm, their ducts feeding the green braziers above.

And I saw the raw material.

They were not mining ore. They were mining memory. Or the vessel of it.

Along one far wall, in row upon row of stone niches, people stood. They were not bound. They simply stood, facing the chamber, their eyes open and unseeing. Their clothes were the ordinary clothes of Kirkwall: fisher’s wool, merchant’s linen, beggar’s rags. Men, women, even a few children, all still as statues. Tubes of clear glass, filled with a swirling, smoke-like substance, ran from temples and chests to a central collection of pulsating, organ-like vats.

The Choir was not just making musicians. They were harvesting listeners. They were drawing the silence out of them, bottling it, feeding it to the green fire that powered their terrible art.

This was the Silence Beneath the Music. This was the source of their power. Not control of sound, but the systematic consumption of attention, of will, of the quiet hum of a conscious mind. They turned people into empty vessels and used their stillness to fuel the tuning of the world.

My breath caught, a ragged, painful thing. I understood now the gaunt man’s words. *Reset the chamber.* They would clear these niches. They would process my father, Fergus, Elspeth, the others. They would add their silence to the vats. And Lucas… Lucas was the Source. He was not a consumer, but a generator. His will, his talent, his very self, was the catalyst that made the harvesting efficient. He was the key that locked the door from the inside.

A movement on the gantry directly opposite my ledge caught my eye. Two grey robes were leading a figure in a plain shift toward an empty niche. The figure walked compliantly, head down. As they turned her, the green firelight fell across her face.

Elspeth.

Her eyes were dull, already half-gone. They guided her into the niche, attached the glass tubes with swift, practised motions. She did not flinch. She settled into the posture of the others, a perfect, vacant stillness descending upon her like a shroud.

Something in me, something that had been holding its breath since the moment I broke the blue thread on the letter, snapped.

I did not shout. A shout would be a note, and a note could be tuned, could be silenced. I made a different sound. A raw, wordless scrape of grief and rage, torn from a place before language. It was not loud, but it was *mine*. Unmeasured. Discordant.

It echoed in the vast space, a tiny, human crack in the monumental silence.

Every grey robe on the nearest gantry stopped. As one, their heads turned toward my ledge. I froze, pressed back into the shadows of my tunnel mouth. For a long moment, nothing happened. They were listening, triangulating the discord.

Then, a bell sounded. Not a church bell, but a flat, atonal chime that seemed to vibrate in my teeth. It was a command in a single note.

On the gantry, the two robes who had attended to Elspeth left her and began to move toward a staircase that connected the walkways to the lower levels of the chamber, levels that likely led toward my ledge. Their movement was not hurried, but inexorable. They had located the error. They were coming to correct it.

I retreated into my tunnel, but the green firelight bled into it now, showing me its true nature. It was not an escape route. It was a service conduit. It would lead me down, into the bowels of the chamber. Into the heart of the machine.

I had a choice: go back the way I came, to the corridor where my father had made his stand, or go forward, into the forge.

Going back was a return to a known defeat. Going forward was a step into an unknown one. But it was a step. It was a term I could introduce myself.

I went forward.

The tunnel became a staircase, carved roughly from the rock, spiralling down along the chamber wall. I took the steps two at a time, my soft boots making no sound. I passed other tunnel mouths, other conduits, all dark. The chamber yawned to my left, a geography of nightmare. The chime sounded again, closer now. The two robes had reached the base of their staircase and were crossing the floor of the chamber, heading for the base of mine.

I ran faster. The staircase ended in a low, arched doorway. Beyond it was not another cavern, but a corridor of finished stone. The air was warmer here, thick with the acrid smell I had noticed before—a chemical reek, like vinegar and hot copper. This was a working level. Conduits of polished wood and glass ran along the ceiling, carrying the smoke-silence from the niches above to the vats below. The green firelight was replaced by the steady, cold glow of enclosed lumen-orbs.

I heard footsteps ahead, the soft, synchronized slap of rope-soled shoes on stone. I ducked into a shallow alcove holding a brass valve wheel as a pair of grey robes passed, their hands clasped, their heads bowed in what looked like meditation. They did not look left or right. Their purpose was internal, their path predetermined.

When they were gone, I moved on. The corridor was a maze of identical passages and humming doors. I was a rat in the walls of the instrument. I had no plan, only a direction: down, and further in. To find the centre of the web. To find Lucas before they reset him, before they completed their equation with my father’s silence.

I found a door that was different. It was not wood, but a slab of dull, grey metal, like the door to a vault. A single, complex lock mechanism was set into its centre, but it stood slightly ajar. From within, I heard a sound that did not fit the sterile hum of this place. It was the sound of a bow, hesitantly drawn across a string.

A violin string.

I pushed the door. It swung inward on silent, oiled hinges.

The room was a cell, but a cell furnished as a workshop. A narrow cot. A table strewn with sheets of musical notation so dense they looked like mathematical formulae. A stand holding a single violin.

And Lucas.

He stood with his back to me, facing the far wall, which was not stone but a single, immense pane of dark, glassy material. It reflected the room dimly. In the reflection, I could see his face. It was empty, smooth as still water. His hands held the violin and bow, but loosely, as if he had forgotten what they were for. He was staring at his own reflection.

On the table beside the notation, I saw it. A square of parchment, a length of dark blue thread beside it. His unfinished letter.

“Lucas.”

The name left my lips as a whisper, a breath of the old air from the workshop, of varnish and cut wood.

He did not turn. But his eyes in the reflection shifted, minutely, from his own image to mine, hovering in the doorway behind him.

“Isabeau.” His voice was flat, drained of all its old music. It was the voice from the corridor, the voice that had called me the variable. “You are not resolved.”

“I’m here to resolve you,” I said, stepping inside. The door sighed shut behind me. The lock clicked. I was trapped with him. The variable was now in the same term as the source.

“I am resolved,” he said. “I am the Source. The equation simplifies through me. All discord flows into my string and is rendered into perfect silence.” He lifted the violin, tucking it under his chin in a motion that was both achingly familiar and utterly alien. “You are the last discord. The final variable.”

He set the bow to the string. He did not play a melody. He played a single, pure note. It was the note I had heard in my dreams, the note of the Haar fog, the note of the empty workshop. It was the sound of absence given form.

The note did not stay in the air. It pressed into it. The room seemed to tighten around the sound, the walls leaning in to listen. My own thoughts began to scatter, to smooth out. The sharp edges of my fear, my grief for my father, my rage at the chamber of niches—they began to blur, to soften into a warm, welcoming nothingness. How easy it would be to let go. To be still. To be silent.

I looked at his reflection. I looked into his black-water eyes. And I saw, deep beneath the surface, a flicker. A strain. The ghost of the boy who had failed to appear for Prime, whose absence was a louder missive than any paper could carry.

He was fighting it. The part of him that had written the letter was still in there, drowning in the silence he was generating.

The note swelled, filling my skull. I could barely remember my own name. I was becoming a constant. I was becoming resolved.

My hand went to my pouch. My fingers found not the ring, but the other thing I carried. The thing I had picked up from the floor of the catacombs, in the moment before I fled. I had not known why I grabbed it. My hand had acted without my mind.

I pulled it out. It was a violin peg. A cheap, simple thing of stained boxwood, carved with a clumsy, looping design. It was the peg from Lucas’s first violin, the one he had smashed in a fit of passion when he was twelve, declaring its tone too common for his genius. I had kept it. A silly keepsake.

I held it up, my arm heavy as stone.

“You broke the G string,” I said, the words thick, wading through the syrup of the silence. “You said it was the string of complacency.”

The perfect note wavered. For a fraction of a second, it frayed.

In the reflection, his eyes widened. The flicker beneath the black water became a struggle.

“You cried,” I pushed on, the memory surfacing through the silencing tide. “After you broke it. You thought Father would disown you for wasting good wood. He didn’t. He made you carve a new peg yourself. You carved this. You said it was ugly. You hated it.”

The note fractured. It became two notes, grating against each other.

Lucas’s bow hand trembled. The violin slipped a fraction from his chin. “Isabeau… stop.”

“You gave it to me,” I said, taking a step forward, the peg held out like a talisman. “You said, ‘Keep this. So you can always remind me I’m an idiot.’” My voice broke. “You are an idiot, Lucas. You ran. And I am running after you. That is the equation. That is the only music that matters.”

The note shattered.

With a cry that was part sob, part roar, Lucas hurled the violin from him. It struck the dark glass wall with a discordant crash of wood and string. He spun to face me, his hands clawing at his own temples. His face was a battlefield. The smooth, alien purpose warred with a torrent of returning self—shame, terror, recognition, love.

“I can’t… I can’t hold it back,” he gasped, falling to his knees. “The music… it’s in the silence. It’s all silence. They showed me. They showed me how to make the world still. How to make the pain stop.”

I was beside him, grabbing his shoulders. “What pain?”

He looked up at me, and for the first time, I saw my brother. Truly saw him. The raw, un-tuned humanity of him. “The workshop,” he whispered. “The dust. The ghost of her in every corner. The way Father looked at me, expecting me to be the bridge, to span the gap she left. I couldn’t bear the weight of it. The noise of all that missing her. I wanted… I wanted a single note. One pure thing. They promised it.”

The door lock disengaged with a heavy *clunk*.

We both looked up.

The gaunt man stood in the doorway. The strip of cloth over his ruined eye was fresh. In his hands, he no longer held Lucas’s violin. He held my father’s iron lever. The end of it was dark with a substance that was not oil.

He looked from Lucas, crumpled on the floor, to me, holding the boxwood peg. His thin lips pressed into a line of disappointment.

“The source is corrupted. The variable has introduced irrationality.” He raised the lever, pointing it at us like a conductor’s baton. “The experiment is concluded. We will salvage the raw materials and begin anew.”

From behind him, two grey robes stepped into the room. They were larger than the others, their silence a physical presence. They moved toward Lucas.

“No!” I threw myself between them and my brother.

The gaunt man sighed, a sound like dry leaves skittering on stone. “You are a stubborn variable. Very well. We will resolve you first.”

He gestured with the lever.

The two robes changed course. Their hands, strong and cold as stone, closed around my arms. I fought, but it was like fighting the tide. They began to drag me toward the door. Toward the chamber of niches.

Lucas stared, paralysed, the war still raging within him.

I met his eyes as they pulled me away. I did not shout. I did not plead. I simply held his gaze and said the last term of our private, sibling calculus.

“Finish your letter.”

His face contorted. The war ended. One side won.

As the grey robes hauled me into the corridor, I heard it. Not a note of silencing music. Not a shout.

A name.

My name.

Roared in Lucas van der Hagen’s own true, furious, beautifully discordant voice.

And then came the sound of splintering wood, and the satisfying, bone-deep crack of an iron lever being wrenched from the hands of a surprised man, followed by a cry that was not silent at all.

The variable had been resolved. It had resolved the source.

And together, we would make a most terrible and beautiful noise.



---

# Chapter 24

## **CHAPTER 24**

The noise we made was not a chord. It was not a harmony. It was the sound a forest makes when it falls—a great, groaning, splintering cacophony of release, each tree finding its own terrible path to the earth.

The gaunt man’s cry was the first note. A short, sharp punctuation of surprise, more air than voice. The iron lever, now in Lucas’s hands, sang its own wet, metallic song as he swung it in a short, vicious arc. The sound it made against the man’s temple was not the crack of bone, but the dull, final *thud* of a mallet driving home the last peg in a coffin lid. The man folded, a marionette with its strings cut, into a heap of grey cloth and sudden stillness.

Silence rushed in to fill the space his presence had occupied. It lasted a heartbeat.

Then the two grey robes holding me moved. Their grip, stone-cold and absolute, did not slacken. They pivoted as one, dragging me backward into the corridor, their purpose unchanged: the chamber of niches, the final silencing. Their master was a variable resolved, but their function remained. They were the final, grinding cogs of a machine that did not know it was broken.

I fought, not like the tide, but like a rat in a sack—all teeth and desperation and pointless fury. My heels scraped on the stone. The boxwood peg was still clutched in my fist, a pathetic weapon. I drove it backward into the robed side of one. There was no gasp, no flinch. It was like stabbing a sack of wet flour.

From the workshop doorway, a shadow detached itself.
